<h1>The White Company</h1>
<h3>By Sir Arthur Conan Doyle</h3>
<h3>Published in 1891. Original text from Gutenberg.org</h3>

<h2>Chapter I. How the Black Sheep Came Forth From the Fold</h2>
The great bell of Beaulieu was ringing. Far away through the forest might be heard its musical clangor and swell. Peat-cutters on Blackdown and fishers upon the Exe heard the distant throbbing rising and falling upon the sultry summer air. It was a common sound in those parts—as common as the chatter of the jays and the booming of the bittern. Yet the fishers and the peasants raised their heads and looked questions at each other, for the angelus had already gone and vespers was still far off. Why should the great bell of Beaulieu toll when the shadows were neither short nor long?
All round the Abbey the {{ monk(Beaulieu) }}s were trooping in. Under the long green-paved avenues of gnarled oaks and of lichened beeches the white-robed {{ brother(Beaulieu) }}s gathered to the sound. From the vine-yard and the vine-press, from the bouvary or ox-farm, from the marl-pits and salterns, even from the distant iron-works of Sowley and the outlying grange of St. Leonard’s, they had all turned their steps homewards. It had been no sudden call. A swift messenger had the night before sped round to the outlying dependencies of the Abbey, and had left the summons for every {{monk(Beaulieu) }} to be back in the cloisters by the third hour after noontide. So urgent a message had not been issued within the memory of old lay-{{ brother(Athanasius) }} {{Athanasius}}, who had cleaned the Abbey knocker since the year after the Battle of Bannockburn.
A stranger who knew nothing either of the Abbey or of its immense resources might have gathered from the appearance of the {{brothers(Beaulieu) }} some conception of the varied duties which they were called upon to perform, and of the busy, wide-spread life which centred in the old monastery. As they swept gravely in by twos and by threes, with bended heads and muttering lips there were few who did not bear upon them some signs of their daily toil. Here were two with wrists and sleeves all spotted with the ruddy grape juice. There again was a tall {{brother(Beaulieu) }} with a broad-headed axe and a bundle of faggots upon {{ hisd(Beaulieu) }} shoulders, while beside {{ him(Beaulieu) }} walked another with the shears under {{ hisd(Beaulieu) }} arm and the white wool still clinging to {{ hisd(Beaulieu) }} whiter gown. A long, straggling troop bore spades and mattocks while the two rearmost of all staggered along under a huge basket o’ fresh-caught carp, for the morrow was Friday, and there were fifty platters to be filled and as many sturdy trenchermen behind them. Of all the throng there was scarce one who was not labor-stained and weary, for {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} Berghersh was a hard {{ man(Berghersh) }} to {{ himself(Berghersh) }} and to others.
Meanwhile, in the broad and lofty chamber set apart for occasions of import, the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} {{ himself(Berghersh) }} was pacing impatiently backwards and forwards, with {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} long white nervous hands clasped in front of {{ him(Berghersh) }}. {{ Hisd(Berghersh) }} thin, thought-worn features and sunken, haggard cheeks bespoke one who had indeed beaten down that inner foe whom every {{ man(Berghersh) }} must face, but had none the less suffered sorely in the contest. In crushing {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} passions {{ he(Berghersh) }} had well-nigh crushed {{ himself(Berghersh) }}. Yet, frail as was {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} person there gleamed out ever and anon from under {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} drooping brows a flash of fierce energy, which recalled to {{ men(Berghersh) }}’s minds that {{ he(Berghersh) }} came of a fighting stock, and that even now {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} twin-{{ brother(Bedivere) }}, {{ Sirr(Bedivere) }} {{Bedivere}} Berghersh, was one of the most famous of those stern warriors who had planted the Cross of St. George before the gates of Paris. With lips compressed and clouded brow, {{ he(Berghersh) }} strode up and down the oaken floor, the very genius and impersonation of asceticism, while the great bell still thundered and clanged above {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} head. At last the uproar died away in three last, measured throbs, and ere their echo had ceased the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} struck a small gong which summoned a lay-{{ brother(Francis) }} to {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} presence.
“Have the {{ brethren(Beaulieu) }} come?” {{ he(Berghersh) }} asked, in the Anglo-French dialect used in religious houses.
“They are here,” the other answered, with {{ hisd(Francis) }} eyes cast down and {{ hisd(Francis) }} hands crossed upon {{ hisd(Francis) }} chest.
“All?”
“Two and thirty of the seniors and fifteen of the novices, most holy {{ father(Berghersh) }}. {{ Brother(Mark) }} {{Mark}} of the Spicarium is sore smitten with a fever and could not come. {{ He(Mark) }} said that—”
“It boots not what {{ he(Mark) }} said. Fever or no, {{ he(Mark) }} should have come at my call. {{ Hisd(Mark) }} spirit must be chastened, as must that of many more in this Abbey. You yourself, {{ brother(Francis) }} {{Francis}}, have twice raised your voice, so it hath come to my ears, when the reader in the refectory hath been dealing with the lives of God’s most blessed saints. What hast thou to say?”
The lay-{{brother(Francis) }} stood meek and silent, with {{ hisd(Francis) }} arms still crossed in front of {{ him(Francis) }}.
“One thousand Aves and as many Credos, said standing with arms outstretched before the shrine of the Virgin, may help thee to remember that the Creator hath given us two ears and but one mouth, as a token that there is twice the work for the one as for the other. Where is the {{master(Jerome) }}of the novices?”
“{{He(Jerome) }} is without, most holy {{ father(Berghersh) }}.”
“Send {{ him(Jerome) }} hither.”
The sandalled feet clattered over the wooden floor, and the iron-bound door creaked upon its hinges. In a few moments it opened again to admit a short square monk with a heavy, composed face and an authoritative manner.
“You have sent for me, holy {{ father(Berghersh) }}?”
“Yes, {{ brother(Jerome) }} {{Jerome}}, I wish that this matter be disposed of with as little scandal as may be, and yet it is needful that the example should be a public one.” The {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} spoke in Latin now, as a language which was more fitted by its age and solemnity to convey the thoughts of two high dignitaries of the order.
“It would, perchance, be best that the novices be not admitted,” suggested the {{master(Jerome) }}. “This mention of an attractive {{ woman(Matilda) }} may turn their minds from their pious meditations to worldly and evil thoughts.”
“Attractive {{ woman(Matilda) }}! Attractive {{ woman(Matilda) }}!” groaned the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }}. “Well has the holy Chrysostom termed them radix malorum. From {{Eeve(Matilda) }} downwards, what good hath come from any {{ woman(Matilda) }}? Who brings the plaint?”
“It is {{ brother(Ambrose) }} {{Ambrose}}.”
“A holy and devout young {{ man(Ambrose) }}.”
“A light and a pattern to every novice.”
“Let the matter be brought to an issue then according to our old-time monastic habit. Bid the chancellor and the sub-chancellor lead in the {{ brothers(Beaulieu) }} according to age, together with {{ brother(John) }} {{John}}, the accused, and {{ brother(Ambrose) }} {{Ambrose}}, the accuser.”
“And the novices?”
“Let them bide in the north alley of the cloisters. Stay! Bid the sub-chancellor send out to them {{Thomas}} the lector to read unto them from the ‘Gesta beati Benedicti.’ It may save them from foolish and pernicious babbling.”
The {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} was left to {{ himself(Berghersh) }} once more, and bent {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} thin gray face over {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} illuminated breviary. So {{ he(Berghersh) }} remained while the senior {{ monk(Beaulieu) }}s filed slowly and sedately into the chamber seating themselves upon the long oaken benches which lined the wall on either side. At the further end, in two high chairs as large as that of the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }}, though hardly as elaborately carved, sat the {{ master(Jerome) }} of the novices and the chancellor, the latter a broad and portly {{priest(chancellor) }}, with dark mirthful eyes. Between them stood a lean, white-faced {{ brother(Ambrose) }} who appeared to be ill at ease, shifting {{ hisd(Ambrose) }} feet from side to side and tapping {{ hisd(Ambrose) }} chin nervously with the long parchment roll which {{ he(Ambrose) }} held in {{ hisd(Ambrose) }} hand. The {{Abbot(Berghersh) }}, from {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} point of vantage, looked down on the two long lines of faces, placid and sun-browned for the most part, with the large bovine eyes and unlined features which told of their easy, unchanging existence. Then {{ he(Berghersh) }} turned {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} eager fiery gaze upon the pale-faced monk who faced {{ him(Berghersh) }}.
“This plaint is thine, as I learn, {{ brother(Ambrose) }} {{Ambrose}},” said {{ he(Berghersh) }}. “May the holy Benedict, patron of our house, be present this day and aid us in our findings! How many counts are there?”
“Three, most holy {{ father(Berghersh) }},” the {{ brother(Ambrose) }} answered in a low and quavering voice.
“Have you set them forth according to rule?”
“They are here set down, most holy {{ father(Berghersh) }}, upon a cantle of sheep-skin.”
“Let the sheep-skin be handed to the chancellor. Bring in {{ brother(John) }} {{John}}, and let {{ him(John) }} hear the plaints which have been urged against {{ him(John) }}.”
At this order a lay-{{brother(Beaulieu) }} swung open the door, and two other lay-{{brothers(Beaulieu) }} entered leading between them a young novice of the order. {{ He(John) }} was a {{ man(John) }} of huge stature, dark-eyed and red-headed, with a peculiar half-humorous, half-defiant expression upon {{ hisd(John) }} bold, well-marked features. {{ Hisd(John) }} {{cowl(John) }} was thrown back upon {{ hisd(John) }} shoulders, and {{ hisd(John) }} gown, unfastened at the top, disclosed a round, sinewy neck, ruddy and corded like the bark of the fir. Thick, muscular arms, protruded from the wide sleeves of {{ hisd(John) }} habit, while {{ hisd(John) }} white shirt, looped up upon one side, gave a glimpse of a huge knotty leg, scarred and torn with the scratches of brambles. With a bow to the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }}, which had in it perhaps more pleasantry than reverence, the novice strode across to the carved prie-dieu which had been set apart for {{ him(John) }}, and stood silent and erect with {{ hisd(John) }} hand upon the gold bell which was used in the private orisons of the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }}’s own household. {{ Hisd(John) }} dark eyes glanced rapidly over the assembly, and finally settled with a grim and menacing twinkle upon the face of {{ hisd(John) }} accuser.
The chancellor rose, and having slowly unrolled the parchment-scroll, proceeded to read it out in a thick and pompous voice, while a subdued rustle and movement among the {{brothers(Beaulieu) }} bespoke the interest with which they followed the proceedings.
“Charges brought upon the second Thursday after the Feast of the Assumption, in the year of our Lord thirteen hundred and sixty-six, against {{ brother(John) }} {{John}}, formerly known as Hordle {{John}}, or {{John}} of Hordle, but now a novice in the holy monastic order of the Cistercians. Read upon the same day at the Abbey of Beaulieu in the presence of the most reverend {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} Berghersh and of the assembled order.
“The charges against the said {{ brother(John) }} {{John}} are the following, namely, to wit:
“First, that on the above-mentioned Feast of the Assumption, small beer having been served to the novices in the proportion of one quart to each four, the said {{ brother(John) }} {{John}} did drain the pot at one draught to the detriment of {{ brother(Paul) }} {{Paul}}, {{ brother(Porphyry) }} Porphyry and {{ brother(Ambrose) }} {{Ambrose}}, who could scarce eat their none-meat of salted stock-fish on account of their exceeding dryness.”
At this solemn indictment the novice raised {{ hisd(John) }} hand and twitched {{ hisd(John) }} lip, while even the placid senior {{brothers(Beaulieu) }} glanced across at each other and coughed to cover their amusement. The {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} alone sat gray and immutable, with a drawn face and a brooding eye.
“Item, that having been told by the {{master(Jerome) }} of the novices that {{ he(John) }} should restrict {{ hisd(John) }} food for two days to a single three-pound loaf of bran and beans, for the greater honoring and glorifying of St. Monica, mother of the holy Augustine, {{ he(John) }} was heard by {{ brother(Ambrose ) }} {{Ambrose}} and others to say that {{ he(John) }} wished twenty thousand devils would fly away with the said Monica, mother of the holy Augustine, or any other saint who came between a {{ man(John) }} and {{ hisd(John) }} food. Item, that upon {{ brother(Ambrose ) }} {{Ambrose}} reproving {{ him(John) }} for this blasphemous wish, {{ he(John) }} did hold the said {{ brother(Ambrose) }} face downwards over the piscatorium or fish-pond for a space during which the said {{ brother(Ambrose) }} was able to repeat a pater and four aves for the better fortifying of {{ hisd(Ambrose) }} soul against impending death.”
There was a buzz and murmur among the white-frocked {{brethren(Beaulieu) }} at this grave charge; but the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} held up {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} long quivering hand. “What then?” said {{ he(Berghersh) }}.
“Item, that between nones and vespers on the feast of James the Less the said {{ brother(John) }} {{John}} was observed upon the Brockenhurst road, near the spot which is known as Hatchett’s Pond in converse with a {{female(Matilda) }}, being a {{maiden(Matilda) }} of the name of {{Matilda}} Sowley, the {{ daughter(Matilda) }} of the {{ King(Edward) }}s verderer. Item, that after sundry japes and jokes the said {{ brother(John) }} {{John}} did assist said {{Matilda}} Sowley and did take {{ herd(Matilda) }} arm and convey {{ herd(Matilda) }} across a stream, to the infinite relish of the devil and the exceeding detriment of {{ hisd(John) }} own soul, which scandalous and wilful falling away was witnessed by three members of our order.”
A dead silence throughout the room, with a rolling of heads and upturning of eyes, bespoke the pious horror of the community.
The {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} drew {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} gray brows low over {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} fiercely questioning eyes.
“Who can vouch for this thing?” {{ he(Berghersh) }} asked.
“That can I,” answered the accuser. “So too can {{ brother(Porphyry) }} {{Porphyry}}, who was with me, and {{ brother(Mark ) }} {{Mark}} of the Spicarium, who hath been so much stirred and inwardly troubled by the sight that {{ he(Mark) }} now lies in a fever through it.”
“And the {{ woman(Matilda) }}?” asked the Abbot. “Did {{ she(Matilda) }} not break into lamentation and woe that a {{ brother(John) }} should so demean {{ himself(John) }}?”
“Nay, {{ she(Matilda) }} smiled upon {{ him(John) }} and thanked {{ him(John) }}. I can vouch it and so can {{ brother(Porphyry) }} {{Porphyry}}.”
“Canst thou?” cried the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }}, in a high, tempestuous tone. “Canst thou so? Hast forgotten that the five-and-thirtieth rule of the order is that in the presence of a {{ woman(Mary) }} the face should be ever averted and the eyes cast down? Hast forgot it, I say? If your eyes were upon your sandals, how came ye to see this smile of which ye prate? A week in your cells, false {{ brethren(Beaulieu) }}, a week of rye-bread and lentils, with double lauds and double matins, may help ye to remembrance of the laws under which ye live.”
At this sudden outflame of wrath the two witnesses sank their faces on to their chests, and sat crushed. The {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} turned {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} angry eyes away from them and bent them upon the accused, who met {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} searching gaze with a firm and composed face.
“What hast thou to say, {{ brother(John) }} {{John}}, upon these weighty things which are urged against you?”
“Little enough, good {{ father(Berghersh) }}, little enough,” said the novice, speaking English with a broad West Saxon drawl. The {{brothers(Beaulieu) }}, who were all English, pricked up their ears at the sound of the homely and yet unfamiliar speech; but the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} flushed red with anger, and struck {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} hand upon the oaken arm of {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} chair.
“What talk is this?” {{ he(Berghersh) }} cried. “Is this a tongue to be used within the walls of an old and well-famed monastery? But grace and learning have ever gone hand in hand, and when one is lost it is needless to look for the other.”
“I know not about that,” said {{ brother(John) }} {{John}}. “I know only that the words come kindly to my mouth, for it was the speech of my fathers before me. Under your favor, I shall either use it now or hold my peace.”
The {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} patted {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} foot and nodded {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} head, as one who passes a point but does not forget it.
“For the matter of the ale,” continued {{ brother(John) }} {{John}}, “I had come in hot from the fields and had scarce got the taste of the thing before mine eye lit upon the bottom of the pot. It may be, too, that I spoke somewhat shortly concerning the bran and the beans, the same being poor provender and unfitted for a {{ man(John) }} of my inches. It is true also that I did lay my hands upon this fool of a {{ brother(Ambrose) }} {{Ambrose}}, though, as you can see, I did {{ him(Ambrose) }} little scathe. As regards the {{maiden(Matilda) }}, too, it is true that I did help {{ her(Matilda) }} over the stream, {{ she(Matilda) }} having on {{ herd(Matilda) }} fine hosen and shoon, whilst I had but my wooden shoes, which could take no hurt from the water. I should have thought shame upon my honor, as well as my {{monk(John) }}hood, if I had held back my hand from {{ her(Mary) }}.” {{ He(John) }} glanced around as {{ he(John) }} spoke with the half-amused look which {{ he(John) }} had worn during the whole proceedings.
“There is no need to go further,” said the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }}. “{{He(John) }} has confessed to all. It only remains for me to portion out the punishment which is due to {{ hisd(John) }} evil conduct.”
{{ He(Berghersh) }} rose, and the two long lines of {{brothers(Beaulieu) }} followed {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} example, looking sideways with scared faces at the angry prelate.
“{{John}} of Hordle,” {{ he(Berghersh) }} thundered, “you have shown yourself during the two months of your novitiate to be a recreant {{monk(John) }}, and one who is unworthy to wear the white garb which is the outer symbol of the spotless spirit. That dress shall therefore be stripped from thee, and thou shalt be cast into the outer world without benefit of clerkship, and without lot or part in the graces and blessings of those who dwell under the care of the Blessed Benedict. Thou shalt come back neither to Beaulieu nor to any of the granges of Beaulieu, and thy name shall be struck off the scrolls of the order.”
The sentence appeared a terrible one to the older {{monks(Beaulieu) }}, who had become so used to the safe and regular life of the Abbey that they would have been as helpless as children in the outer world. From their pious oasis they looked dreamily out at the desert of life, a place full of stormings and strivings—comfortless, restless, and overshadowed by evil. The young novice, however, appeared to have other thoughts, for {{ hisd(John) }} eyes sparkled and {{ hisd(John) }} smile broadened. It needed but that to add fresh fuel to the fiery mood of the prelate.
“So much for thy spiritual punishment,” {{ he(Berghersh) }} cried. “But it is to thy grosser feelings that we must turn in such natures as thine, and as thou art no longer under the shield of holy church there is the less difficulty. Ho there! lay-brothers—{{Francis}}, Naomi, Joseph—seize {{ him(John) }} and bind {{ hisd(John) }} arms! Drag {{ him(John) }} forth, and let the foresters and the porters scourge {{ him(John) }} from the precincts!”
As these three lay-brothers advanced towards {{ him(John) }} to carry out the {{Abbot(Berghersh) }}’s direction, the smile faded from the novice’s face, and {{ he(John) }} glanced right and left with {{ hisd(John) }} fierce brown eyes, like a bull at a baiting. Then, with a sudden deep-chested shout, {{ he(John) }} tore up the heavy oaken prie-dieu and poised it to strike, taking two steps backward the while, that none might take {{ him(John) }} at a vantage.
“By the black rood of Waltham!” {{ he(John) }} roared, “if any knave among you lays a finger-end upon the edge of my gown, I will crush your skull like a filbert!” With {{ hisd(John) }} thick knotted arms, {{ hisd(John) }} thundering voice, and {{ hisd(John) }} bristle of red hair, there was something so repellent in the {{ man(John) }} that the three lay-brothers flew back at the very glare of {{ him(John) }}; and the two rows of white {{monks(Beaulieu) }} strained away from {{ him((Beaulieu) }}) }} like poplars in a tempest. The {{Abbot(Berghersh) }} only sprang forward with shining eyes; but the chancellor and the {{master(Jerome) }} hung upon either arm and wrested {{ him(John) }} back out of danger’s way.
“{{He(John) }} is possessed of a devil!” they shouted. “Run, {{ brother(Ambrose) }} {{Ambrose}}, brother Joachim! Call Hugh of the Mill, and Woodman Wat, and Raoul with his arbalest and bolts. Tell them that we are in fear of our lives! Run, run! for the love of the Virgin!”
But the novice was a strategist as well as a {{ man(John) }} of action. Springing forward, {{ he(John) }} hurled {{ hisd(John) }} unwieldy weapon at {{ brother(Ambrose) }} {{Ambrose}}, and, as desk and {{monk(Ambrose) }} clattered on to the floor together, {{ he(John) }} sprang through the open door and down the winding stair. Sleepy old {{ brother(Athanasius) }} Athanasius, at the porter’s cell, had a fleeting vision of twinkling feet and flying skirts; but before {{ he(Athanasius) }} had time to rub {{ hisd(Athanasius) }} eyes the recreant had passed the lodge, and was speeding as fast as {{ hisd(John) }} sandals could patter along the Lyndhurst Road.
<h2>Chapter II. How {{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }} Came Out Into The World</h2>
Never had the peaceful atmosphere of the old Cistercian house been so rudely ruffled. Never had there been insurrection so sudden, so short, and so successful. Yet the {{ Abbot(Berghersh ) }} {{Berghersh}} was a {{ man(Berghersh ) }} of too firm a grain to allow one bold outbreak to imperil the settled order of {{ hisd(Berghersh ) }} great household. In a few hot and bitter words, {{ he(Berghersh ) }} compared their false {{ brother(John) }}’s exit to the expulsion of our first parents from the garden, and more than hinted that unless a reformation occurred some others of the community might find themselves in the same evil and perilous case. Having thus pointed the moral and reduced {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} flock to a fitting state of docility, {{ he(Berghersh) }} dismissed them once more to their labors and withdrew {{ himself(Berghersh) }} to {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} own private chamber, there to seek spiritual aid in the discharge of the duties of {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} high office.
The {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} was still on {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} knees, when a gentle tapping at the door of {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} cell broke in upon {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} orisons.
Rising in no very good humor at the interruption, {{ he(Berghersh) }} gave the word to enter; but {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} look of impatience softened down into a pleasant and {{paternal(Berghersh) }} smile as {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} eyes fell upon {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} visitor.
{{ He(Alleyne) }} was a thin-faced, yellow-haired {{ youth(Alleyne) }}, rather above the middle size, comely and well shapen, with straight, lithe figure and eager, boyish features. {{ Hisd(Alleyne) }} clear, pensive gray eyes, and quick, delicate expression, spoke of a nature which had unfolded far from the boisterous joys and sorrows of the world. Yet there was a set of the mouth and a prominence of the chin which relieved {{ him(Alleyne) }} of any trace of softness. Impulsive {{ he(Alleyne) }} might be, enthusiastic, sensitive, with something sympathetic and adaptive in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} disposition; but an observer of nature’s tokens would have confidently pledged {{ himself(Alleyne) }} that there was native firmness and strength underlying {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} gentle, monastery-bred ways.
The {{ youth(Alleyne) }} was not clad in monastic garb, but in lay attire, though {{ hisd(Alleyne) }}  {{jerkin(Alleyne) }}, tunic and cloak were all of a sombre hue, as befitted one who dwelt in sacred precincts. A broad leather strap hanging from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} shoulder supported a scrip or satchel such as travellers were wont to carry. In one hand {{ he(Alleyne) }} grasped a thick staff pointed and shod with metal, while in the other {{ he(Alleyne) }} held {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} coif or bonnet, which bore in its front a broad pewter medal stamped with the image of Our Lady of Rocamadour.
“Art ready, then, fair {{ son(Alleyne) }}?” said the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }}. “This is indeed a day of comings and of goings. It is strange that in one twelve hours the Abbey should have cast off its foulest weed and should now lose what we are fain to look upon as our choicest blossom.”
“You speak too kindly, {{ father(Berghersh) }},” the {{ youth(Alleyne) }} answered. “If I had my will I should never go forth, but should end my days here in Beaulieu. It hath been my home as far back as my mind can carry me, and it is a sore thing for me to have to leave it.”
“Life brings many a cross,” said the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} gently. “Who is without them? Your going forth is a grief to us as well as to yourself. But there is no help. I had given my foreword and sacred promise to your {{ father(Edric) }}, {{Edric}} the Franklin, that at the age of twenty you should be sent out into the world to see for yourself how you liked the savor of it. Seat thee upon the settle, {{Alleyne}}, for you may need rest ere long.”
The {{ youth(Alleyne) }} sat down as directed, but reluctantly and with diffidence. The {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} stood by the narrow window, and {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} long black shadow fell slantwise across the rush-strewn floor.
“Twenty years ago,” {{ he(Berghersh) }} said, “your {{ father(Edric) }}, the Franklin of Minstead, died, leaving to the Abbey three hides of rich land in the hundred of Malwood, and leaving to us also {{ hisd(Edric) }} infant {{ son(Alleyne) }} on condition that we should rear {{ him(Alleyne) }} until {{ he(Alleyne) }} came to {{ man(Alleyne) }}’s estate. This {{ he(Edric) }} did partly because your other parent was dead, and partly because your elder {{ brother(Simon) }}, now Socman of Minstead, had already given sign of that fierce and rude nature which would make {{ him(Simon) }} no fit companion for you. It was {{ hisd(Edric) }} desire and request, however, that you should not remain in the cloisters, but should at a ripe age return into the world.”
“But, {{ father(Berghersh) }},” interrupted the young {{ man(Alleyne) }}, “it is surely true that I am already advanced several degrees in clerkship?”
“Yes, fair {{ son(Alleyne) }}, but not so far as to bar you from the garb you now wear or the life which you must now lead. You have been porter?”
“Yes, {{ father(Berghersh) }}.”
“Exorcist?”
“Yes, {{ father(Berghersh) }}.”
“Reader?”
“Yes, {{ father(Berghersh) }}.”
“Acolyte?”
“Yes, {{ father(Berghersh) }}.”
“But have sworn no vow of constancy or chastity?”
“No, {{ father(Berghersh) }}.”
“Then you are free to follow a worldly life. But let me hear, ere you start, what gifts you take away with you from Beaulieu? Some I already know. There is the playing of the citole and the rebeck. Our choir will be dumb without you. You carve too?”
The {{ youth(Alleyne) }}’s pale face flushed with the pride of the skilled workman. “Yes, holy {{ father(Berghersh) }},” {{ he(Alleyne) }} answered. “Thanks to good {{ brother(Bartholomew) }} {{Bartholomew}}, I carve in wood and in ivory, and can do something also in silver and in bronze. From {{ brother(Francis ) }} {{Francis}} I have learned to paint on vellum, on glass, and on metal, with a knowledge of those pigments and essences which can preserve the color against damp or a biting air. {{ Brother(Luke) }} {{Luke}} hath given me some skill in damask work, and in the enamelling of shrines, tabernacles, diptychs and triptychs. For the rest, I know a little of the making of covers, the cutting of precious stones, and the fashioning of instruments.”
“A goodly list, truly,” cried the superior with a smile. “What clerk of Cambrig or of Oxenford could say as much? But of thy reading—hast not so much to show there, I fear?”
“No, {{ father(Berghersh) }}, it hath been slight enough. Yet, thanks to our good chancellor, I am not wholly unlettered. I have read Ockham, Bradwardine, and other of the schoolmen, together with the learned Duns Scotus and the book of the holy Aquinas.”
“But of the things of this world, what have you gathered from your reading? From this high window you may catch a glimpse over the wooden point and the smoke of Bucklershard of the mouth of the Exe, and the shining sea. Now, I pray you, {{Alleyne}}, if one were to take a ship and spread sail across yonder waters, where might he hope to arrive?”
The {{ youth(Alleyne) }} pondered, and drew a plan amongst the rushes with the point of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} staff. “Holy {{ father(Berghersh) }},” said {{ he(Alleyne) }}, “one would come upon those parts of France which are held by the {{ King(Edward) }}'s Majesty. But if one trended to the south one might reach Spain and the Barbary States. To the north would be Flanders and the country of the Eastlanders and of the Muscovites.”
“True. And how if, after reaching the {{ King(Edward) }}'s possessions, one still journeyed on to the eastward?”
“The traveler would then come upon that part of France which is still in dispute, and one might hope to reach the famous city of Avignon, where dwells our blessed father, the prop of Christendom.”
“And then?”
“Then he would pass through the land of the Almains and the great Roman Empire, and so to the country of the Huns and of the Lithuanian pagans, beyond which lies the great city of Constantine and the kingdom of the unclean followers of Mahmoud.”
“And beyond that, fair {{ son(Alleyne) }}?”
“Beyond that is Jerusalem and the Holy Land, and the great river which hath its source in the Garden of Eden.”
“And then?”
“Nay, good {{ father(Berghersh) }}, I cannot tell. Methinks the end of the world is not far from there.”
“Then we can still find something to teach thee, {{Alleyne}},” said the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} complaisantly. “Know that many strange nations lie betwixt there and the end of the world. There is the country of the Amazons, and the country of the dwarfs, and the country of the fair but evil women who slay with beholding, like the basilisk. Beyond that again is the kingdom of Prester John and of the great Cham. These things I know for very sooth, for I had them from that pious Christian and valiant knight, Sir John de Mandeville, who stopped twice at Beaulieu on his way to and from Southampton, and discoursed to us concerning what he had seen from the reader’s desk in the refectory, until there was many a good {{ brother(Beaulieu) }} who got neither bit nor sup, so stricken were they by his strange tales.”
“I would fain know, {{ father(Berghersh) }},” asked the young {{ man(Alleyne) }}, “what there may be at the end of the world?”
“There are some things,” replied the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} gravely, “into which it was never intended that we should inquire. But you have a long road before you. Whither will you first turn?”
“To my {{ brother(Simon) }} at Minstead. If {{ he(Simon) }} be indeed an ungodly and violent {{ man(Simon) }}, there is the more need that I should seek {{ him(Simon) }} out and see whether I cannot turn {{ him(Simon) }} to better ways.”
The {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} shook {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} head. “The Socman of Minstead hath earned an evil name over the country side,” {{ he(Berghersh) }} said. “If you must go to {{ him(Simon) }}, see at least that {{ he(Simon) }} doth not turn you from the narrow path upon which you have learned to tread. But you are in God’s keeping, and Godward should you ever look in danger and in trouble. Above all, shun the snares of the lusty, for they are ever set for the foolish feet of the young. Kneel down, my child, and take an old {{ man(Berghersh) }}’s blessing.”
{{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }} bent {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} head while the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} poured out {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} heartfelt supplication that Heaven would watch over this young soul, now going forth into the darkness and danger of the world. It was no mere form for either of them. To them the outside life of mankind did indeed seem to be one of violence and of sin, beset with physical and still more with spiritual danger. Heaven, too, was very near to them in those days. God’s direct agency was to be seen in the thunder and the rainbow, the whirlwind and the lightning. To the believer, clouds of angels and confessors, and martyrs, armies of the sainted and the saved, were ever stooping over their struggling brethren upon earth, raising, encouraging, and supporting them. It was then with a lighter heart and a stouter courage that the young {{ man(Alleyne) }} turned from the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }}’s room, while the latter, following {{ him(Berghersh) }} to the stair-head, finally commended {{ him(Berghersh) }} to the protection of the holy Julian, patron of travellers.
Underneath, in the porch of the Abbey, the {{monks(Beaulieu) }} had gathered to give {{ him(Alleyne) }} a last God-speed. Many had brought some parting token by which {{ he(Alleyne) }} should remember them. There was {{ brother(Bartholomew) }} {{Bartholomew}} with a crucifix of rare carved ivory, and {{ brother(Luke) }} {{Luke}} with a white-backed psalter adorned with golden bees, and {{ brother(Francis) }} {{Francis}} with the “Slaying of the Innocents” most daintily set forth upon vellum. All these were duly packed away deep in the traveller’s scrip, and above them old pippin-faced {{ brother(Athanasius) }} {{Athanasius}} had placed a parcel of simnel bread and rammel cheese, with a small flask of the famous blue-sealed Abbey wine. So, amid hand-shakings and laughings and blessings, {{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }} turned {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} back upon Beaulieu.
At the turn of the road {{ he(Alleyne) }} stopped and gazed back. There was the wide-spread building which {{ he(Alleyne) }} knew so well, the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }}’s house, the long church, the cloisters with their line of arches, all bathed and mellowed in the evening sun. There too was the broad sweep of the river Exe, the old stone well, the canopied niche of the Virgin, and in the centre of all the cluster of white-robed figures who waved their hands to {{ him(Alleyne) }}. A sudden mist swam up before the young {{ man(Alleyne) }}’s eyes, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} turned away upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} journey with a heavy heart and a choking throat.
<h2>Chapter III. How Hordle {{John}} Cozened the Fuller of Lymington</h2>
It is not, however, in the nature of things that a {{ lad(Alleyne) }} of twenty, with young life glowing in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} veins and all the wide world before {{ him(Alleyne) }}, should spend {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} first hours of freedom in mourning for what {{ he(Alleyne) }} had left. Long ere {{Alleyne}} was out of sound of the Beaulieu bells {{ he(Alleyne) }} was striding sturdily along, swinging {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} staff and whistling as merrily as the birds in the thicket. It was an evening to raise one’s heart. The sun shining slantwise through the trees threw delicate traceries across the road, with bars of golden light between. Away in the distance before and behind, the green boughs, now turning in places to a coppery redness, shot their broad arches across the track. The still summer air was heavy with the resinous smell of the great forest. Here and there a tawny brook prattled out from among the underwood and lost itself again in the ferns and brambles upon the further side. Save the dull piping of insects and the sough of the leaves, there was silence everywhere—the sweet restful silence of nature.
And yet there was no want of life—the whole wide wood was full of it. Now it was a lithe, furtive stoat which shot across the path upon some fell errand of its own; then it was a wild cat which squatted upon the outlying branch of an oak and peeped at the traveller with a yellow and dubious eye. Once it was a wild sow which scuttled out of the bracken, with two young sounders at her heels, and once a lordly red staggard walked daintily out from among the tree trunks, and looked around him with the fearless gaze of one who lived under the {{ King(Edward) }}’s own high protection. {{Alleyne}} gave {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} staff a merry flourish, however, and the red deer bethought him that the {{ King(Edward) }} was far off, so streaked away from whence he came.
The {{ youth(Alleyne) }} had now journeyed considerably beyond the furthest domains of the Abbey. {{ He(Alleyne) }} was the more surprised therefore when, on coming round a turn in the path, {{ he(Alleyne) }} perceived a {{ man(John) }} clad in the familiar garb of the order, and seated in a clump of heather by the roadside. {{Alleyne}} had known every {{ brother(John) }} well, but this was a face which was new to {{ him(Alleyne) }}—a face which was very red and puffed, working this way and that, as though the {{ man(John) }} were sore perplexed in {{ hisd(John) }} mind. Once {{ he(John) }} shook both hands furiously in the air, and twice {{ he(John) }} sprang from {{ hisd(John) }} seat and hurried down the road. When {{ he(John) }} rose, however, {{Alleyne}} observed that {{ hisd(John) }} robe was much too long and loose for {{ him(John) }} in every direction, trailing upon the ground and bagging about {{ hisd(John) }} ankles, so that even with trussed-up skirts {{ he(John) }} could make little progress. {{ He(John) }} ran once, but the long gown clogged {{ him(John) }} so that {{ he(John) }} slowed down into a shambling walk, and finally plumped into the heather once more.
“Young friend,” said {{ he(John) }}, when {{Alleyne}} was abreast of {{ him(John) }}, “I fear from thy garb that thou canst know little of the Abbey of Beaulieu.”
“Then you are in error, friend,” the clerk answered, “for I have spent all my days within its walls.”
“Hast so indeed?” cried {{ he(John) }}. “Then perhaps canst tell me the name of a great loathly lump of a {{ brother(John) }} wi’ freckled face an’ a hand like a spade. {{ Hisd(John) }} eyes were black an’ {{ hisd(John) }} hair was red an’ {{ hisd(John) }} voice like the parish bull. I trow that there cannot be two alike in the same cloisters.”
“That surely can be no other than {{ brother(John) }} {{John}},” said {{Alleyne}}. “I trust {{ he(John) }} has done you no wrong, that you should be so hot against {{ him(John) }}.”
“Wrong, quotha?” cried the other, jumping out of the heather. “Wrong! why {{ he(John) }} hath stolen every plack of clothing off my back, if that be a wrong, and hath left me here in this sorry frock of white falding, so that I have shame to go back to my family, lest they think that I have donned this old kirtle. Harrow and alas that ever I should have met {{ him(John) }}!”
“But how came this?” asked the young clerk, who could scarce keep from laughter at the sight of the hot little {{ man(John) }} so swathed in the great white cloak.
“It came in this way,” {{ he(John) }} said, sitting down once more: “I was passing this way, hoping to reach Lymington ere nightfall when I came on this red-headed {{ knave(John) }} seated even where we are sitting now. I uncovered and louted as I passed thinking that {{ he(John) }} might be a holy {{ man(John) }} at {{ hisd(John) }} orisons, but {{ he(John) }} called to me and asked me if I had heard speak of the new indulgence in favor of the Cistercians. ‘Not I,’ I answered. ‘Then the worse for thy soul!’ said {{ he(John) }}; and with that {{ he(John) }} broke into a long tale how that on account of the virtues of the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} Berghersh it had been decreed by the Pope that whoever should wear the habit of a {{ monk(John) }} of Beaulieu for as long as {{ he(John) }} might say the seven psalms of David should be assured of the kingdom of Heaven. When I heard this I prayed {{ him(John) }} on my knees that {{ he(John) }} would give me the use of {{ hisd(John) }} gown, which after many contentions {{ he(John) }} at last agreed to do, on my paying {{ him(John) }} three marks towards the regilding of the image of Laurence the martyr. Having stripped {{ hisd(John) }} robe, I had no choice but to let {{ him(John) }} have the wearing of my good leathern {{ jerkin(John) }} and tunic, for, as {{ he(John) }} said, it was chilling to the blood and unseemly to the eye to stand frockless whilst I made my orisons. {{ He(John) }} had scarce got them on, and it was a sore labor, seeing that my inches will scarce match my girth—he had scarce got them on, I say, and I not yet at the end of the second psalm, when {{ he(John) }} bade me do honor to my new dress, and with that set off down the road as fast as feet would carry {{ him(John) }}. For myself, I could no more run than if I had been sown in a sack; so here I sit, and here I am like to sit, before I set eyes upon my clothes again.”
“Nay, friend, take it not so sadly,” said {{Alleyne}}, clapping the disconsolate one upon the shoulder. “Canst change thy robe for proper clothes once more at the Abbey, unless perchance you have a friend near at hand.”
“That have I,” {{ he(John) }} answered, “and close; but I care not to go nigh {{ him(John) }} in this plight, for {{ hisd(John) }} spouse hath a gibing tongue, and will spread the tale until I could not show my face in any market from Fordingbridge to Southampton. But if you, fair {{ ssir(Alleyne) }}, out of your kind charity would be pleased to go a matter of two bow-shots out of your way, you would do me such a service as I could scarce repay.”
“With all my heart,” said {{Alleyne}} readily.
“Then take this pathway on the left, I pray thee, and then the deer-track which passes on the right. You will then see under a great beech-tree the hut of a charcoal-burner. Give {{ him(John) }} my name, good {{ ssir(Alleyne) }}, the name of Petra the fuller, of Lymington, and ask {{ him(John) }} for a change of raiment, that I may pursue my journey without delay. There are reasons why {{ he(John) }} would be loth to refuse me.”
{{Alleyne}} started off along the path indicated, and soon found the log-hut where the burner dwelt. The man was away faggot-cutting in the forest, but his wife, a ruddy bustling dame, found the needful garments and tied them into a bundle. While she busied herself in finding and folding them, {{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }} stood by the open door looking in at her with much interest and some distrust, for {{ he(Alleyne) }} had never been so nigh to a married woman before. She had round red arms, a dress of some sober woollen stuff, and a brass brooch the size of a cheese-cake stuck in the front of it.
“Petra the fuller!” she kept repeating. “Marry come up! if I were Petra the fuller’s mother I would teach {{ him(John) }} better than to give {{ hisd(John) }} clothes to the first rogue who asks for them. But {{ he(John) }} was always a poor, fond, silly creature, was Petra, though we are beholden to {{ him(John) }} for helping to bury our second child Wat, who was a ‘prentice to {{ him(John) }} at Lymington in the year of the Black Death. But who are you, young {{ ssir(Alleyne) }}?”
“I am a clerk on my road from Beaulieu to Minstead.”
“Aye, indeed! Hast been brought up at the Abbey then. I could read it from thy reddened cheek and downcast eye. Hast learned from the {{monks(Beaulieu) }}, I trow, to fear a goodwife as thou wouldst a lazar-house. Out upon them! that they should dishonor their own mothers by such teaching. A pretty world it would be with all the wives out of it.”
“Heaven forfend that such a thing should come to pass!” said {{Alleyne}}.
“Amen and amen! But thou art a pretty {{ lad(Alleyne) }}, and the prettier for thy modest ways. It is easy to see from thy cheek that thou hast not spent thy days in the rain and the heat and the wind, as my poor Wat hath been forced to do.”
“I have indeed seen little of life, good dame.”
“Wilt find nothing in it to pay for the loss of thy own freshness. Here are the clothes, and Petra can leave them when next {{ he(John) }} comes this way. Holy Virgin! see the dust upon thy clothes! It were easy to see that there is no mother to tend to thee. So!—that is better. Now buss me, {{ boy(Alleyne) }}.”
{{Alleyne}} stooped and kissed her, for the kiss was the common salutation of the age, and, as Erasmus long afterwards remarked, more used in England than in any other country. Yet it sent the blood to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} temples again, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} wondered, as {{ he(Alleyne) }} turned away, what the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} {{Berghersh}} would have answered to so frank an invitation. {{ He(Alleyne) }} was still tingling from this new experience when {{ he(Alleyne) }} came out upon the high-road and saw a sight which drove all other thoughts from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} mind.
Some way down from where {{ he(Alleyne) }} had left {{ him(John) }} the unfortunate Petra was stamping and raving tenfold worse than before. Now, however, instead of the great white cloak, {{ he(John) }} had no clothes on at all, save a short woollen tunic and a pair of leather shoes. Far down the road a long-legged figure was running, with a bundle under one arm and the other hand to {{ hisd(John) }} side, like a {{ man(John) }} who laughs until {{ he(John) }} is sore.
“See {{ him(John) }}!” yelled Petra. “Look to {{ him(John) }}! You shall be my witness. {{ He(John) }} shall see Winchester jail for this. See where {{ he(John) }} goes with my cloak under {{ hisd(John) }} arm!”
“Who then?” cried {{Alleyne}}.
“Who but that cursed {{ brother(John) }} {{John}}. {{ He(John) }} hath not left me clothes enough to make a gallybagger. The double thief hath cozened me out of my gown.”
“Stay though, my friend, it was {{ hisd(John) }} gown,” objected {{Alleyne}}.
“It boots not. {{ He(John) }} hath them all—gown, {{ jerkin(John) }}, hosen and all. Gramercy to {{ him(John) }} that {{ he(John) }} left me shoon. I doubt not that {{ he(John) }} will be back for them anon.”
“But how came this?” asked {{Alleyne}}, open-eyed with astonishment.
“Are those the clothes? For dear charity’s sake give them to me. Not the Pope himself shall have these from me, though he sent the whole college of cardinals to ask it. How came it? Why, you had scarce gone ere this loathly {{John}} came running back again, and, when I oped mouth to reproach {{ him(John) }}, {{ he(John) }} asked me whether it was indeed likely that a {{ man(John) }} of prayer would leave {{ hisd(John) }} own godly raiment in order to take a layman’s clothes. {{ He(John) }} had, {{ he(John) }} said, but gone for a while that I might be the freer for my devotions. On this I plucked off the gown, and {{ he(John) }} with much show of haste did begin to undo {{ hisd(John) }} points; but when I threw {{ hisd(John) }} frock down {{ he(John) }} clipped it up and ran off all untrussed, leaving me in this sorry plight. {{ He(John) }} laughed so the while, like a great croaking frog, that I might have caught {{ him(John) }} had my breath not been as short as {{ hisd(John) }} legs were long.”
The young {{ man(Alleyne) }} listened to this tale of wrong with all the seriousness that {{ he(Alleyne) }} could maintain; but at the sight of the pursy red-faced {{ man(John) }} and the dignity with which {{ he(John) }} bore {{ him(John) }}, the laughter came so thick upon {{ him(Alleyne) }} that {{ he(Alleyne) }} had to lean up against a tree-trunk. The fuller looked sadly and gravely at {{ him(Alleyne) }}; but finding that {{ he(Alleyne) }} still laughed, {{ he(John) }} bowed with much mock politeness and stalked onwards in {{ hisd(John) }} borrowed clothes. {{Alleyne}} watched {{ him(John) }} until {{ he(John) }} was small in the distance, and then, wiping the tears from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} eyes, {{ he(Alleyne) }} set off briskly once more upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} journey.
<h2>Chapter IV.  How the bailiff of Southampton slew the two masterless tumblers</h2>
The road along which {{ he(Alleyne) }} travelled was scarce as populous as most other roads in the kingdom, and far less so than those which lie between the larger towns. Yet from time to time {{Alleyne}} met other wayfarers, and more than once was overtaken by strings of pack mules and horsemen journeying in the same direction as {{ himself(Alleyne) }}. Once a begging friar came limping along in a brown habit, imploring in a most dolorous voice to give him a single groat to buy bread wherewith to save himself from impending death. {{Alleyne}} passed him swiftly by, for {{ he(Alleyne) }} had learned from the {{ monks(Beaulieu) }} to have no love for the wandering friars, and, besides, there was a great half-gnawed mutton bone sticking out of pouch to prove him a liar. Swiftly as {{ he(Alleyne) }} went, however, {{ he(Alleyne) }} could not escape the curse of the four blessed evangelists which the mendicant howled behind him. So dreadful are his execrations that the frightened {{ lad(Alleyne) }} thrust {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} fingers into {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} ear-holes, and ran until the fellow was but a brown smirch upon the yellow road.
Further on, at the edge of the woodland, {{ he(Alleyne) }} came upon a chap{{ man(chapman) }} and {{ hisd(chapman) }} {{ wife(partner) }}, who sat upon a fallen tree. {{ He(chapman) }} had put {{ hisd(chapman) }} pack down as a table, and the two of them were devouring a great pasty, and washing it down with some drink from a stone jar. The {{chapman}} broke a rough jest as {{ he(chapman) }} passed, and the {{ woman(partner) }} called shrilly to {{Alleyne}} to come and join them, on which the {{ man(chapman) }}, turning suddenly from mirth to wrath, began to belabor {{ her(partner) }} with {{ hisd(chapman) }} cudgel. {{Alleyne}} hastened on, lest {{ he(chapman) }} make more mischief, and {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} heart was heavy as lead within {{ him(Alleyne) }}. Look where {{ he(Alleyne) }} would, {{ he(Alleyne) }} seemed to see nothing but injustice and violence and the hardness of men and women.
But even as {{ he(Alleyne) }} brooded sadly over it and pined for the sweet peace of the Abbey, {{ he(Alleyne) }} came on an open space dotted with holly bushes, where was the strangest sight that {{ he(Alleyne) }} had yet chanced upon. Near to the pathway lay a long clump of greenery, and from behind this there stuck straight up into the air four human legs clad in parti-colored hosen, yellow and black. Strangest of all was when a brisk tune struck suddenly up and the four legs began to kick and twitter in time to the music. Walking on tiptoe round the bushes, {{ he(Alleyne) }} stood in amazement to see two people bounding about on their heads, while they played, the one a viol and the other a pipe, as merrily and as truly as though they were seated in a choir. {{Alleyne}} crossed {{ himself(Alleyne) }} as {{ he(Alleyne) }} gazed at this unnatural sight, and could scarce hold {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} ground with a steady face, when the two dancers, catching sight of {{ him(Alleyne) }}, came bouncing in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} direction. A spear’s length from {{ him(Alleyne) }}, they each threw a somersault into the air, and came down upon their feet with smirking faces and their hands over their hearts.
“A guerdon—a guerdon, my {{knight(tumbler2) }}of the staring eyes!” cried one.
“A gift, my {{ prince(tumbler1) }}!” shouted the other. “Any trifle will serve—a purse of gold, or even a jewelled goblet.”
{{Alleyne}} thought of what {{ he(Alleyne) }} had read of demoniac possession—the jumpings, the twitchings, the wild talk. It was in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} mind to repeat over the exorcism proper to such attacks; but the two burst out a-laughing at {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} scared face, and turning on to their heads once more, clapped their heels in derision.
“Hast never seen tumblers before?” asked the elder, a black-browed, swarthy {{ man(tumbler2) }}, as brown and supple as a hazel twig. “Why shrink from us, then, as though we were the spawn of the Evil One?”
“Why shrink, my honey-bird? Why so afeard, my sweet cinnamon?” exclaimed the other, a loose-jointed lanky {{ youth(tumbler1) }} with a dancing, roguish eye.
“Truly, it is a new sight to me,” the clerk answered. “When I saw your four legs above the bush I could scarce credit my own eyes. Why is it that you do this thing?”
“A dry question to answer,” cried the younger, coming back on to {{ hisd(tumbler1) }} feet. “A most husky question, my fair bird! But how? A flask, a flask!—by all that is wonderful!” {{ He(tumbler1) }} shot out {{ hisd(tumbler1) }} hand as {{ he(tumbler1) }} spoke, and plucking {{Alleyne}}’s bottle out of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} scrip, {{ he(tumbler1) }} deftly knocked the neck off, and poured the half of it down {{ hisd(tumbler1) }} throat. The rest {{ he(tumbler1) }} handed to {{ hisd(tumbler1) }} comrade, who drank the wine, and then, to the clerk’s increasing amazement, made a show of swallowing the bottle, with such skill that {{Alleyne}} seemed to see it vanish down {{ hisd(tumbler2) }} throat. A moment later, however, {{ he(tumbler2) }} flung it over {{ hisd(tumbler2) }} head, and caught it bottom downwards upon the calf of {{ hisd(tumbler2) }} left leg.
“We thank you for the wine, kind {{ ssir(Alleyne) }},” said {{ he(tumbler2) }}, “and for the ready courtesy wherewith you offered it. Touching your question, we may tell you that we are strollers and jugglers, who, having performed with much applause at Winchester fair, are now on our way to the great Michaelmas market at Ringwood. As our art is a very fine and delicate one, however, we cannot let a day go by without exercising ourselves in it, to which end we choose some quiet and sheltered spot where we may break our journey. Here you find us; and we cannot wonder that you, who are new to tumbling, should be astounded, since many great barons, earls, marshals and knights, who have wandered as far as the Holy Land, are of one mind in saying that they have never seen a more noble or gracious performance. If you will be pleased to sit upon that stump, we will now continue our exercise.”
{{Alleyne}} sat down willingly as directed with two great bundles on either side of {{ him(Alleyne) }} which contained the strollers’ dresses—doublets of flame-colored silk and girdles of leather, spangled with brass and tin. The jugglers were on their heads once more, bounding about with rigid necks, playing the while in perfect time and tune. It chanced that out of one of the bundles there stuck the end of what the clerk saw to be a cittern, so drawing it forth, {{ he(Alleyne) }} tuned it up and twanged a harmony to the merry lilt which the dancers played. On that they dropped their own instruments, and putting their hands to the ground they hopped about faster and faster, ever shouting to {{ him(Alleyne) }} to play more briskly, until at last for very weariness all three had to stop.
“Well played, sweet poppet!” cried the younger. “Hast a rare touch on the strings.”
“How knew you the tune?” asked the other.
“I knew it not. I did but follow the notes I heard.”
Both opened their eyes at this, and stared at {{Alleyne}} with as much amazement as {{ he(Alleyne) }} had shown at them.
“You have a fine trick of ear then,” said one. “We have long wished to meet such a {{ man(Alleyne) }}. Wilt join us and jog on to Ringwood? Thy duties shall be light, and thou shalt have two-pence a day and meat for supper every night.”
“With as much beer as you can put away,” said the other, “and a flask of Gascon wine on Sabbaths.”
“Nay, it may not be. I have other work to do. I have tarried with you over long,” quoth {{Alleyne}}, and resolutely set forth upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} journey once more. They ran behind {{ him(Alleyne) }} some little way, offering {{ him(Alleyne) }} first fourpence and then sixpence a day, but {{ he(Alleyne) }} only smiled and shook {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} head, until at last they fell away from {{ him(Alleyne) }}. Looking back, {{ he(Alleyne) }} saw that the smaller had mounted on the younger’s shoulders, and that they stood so, some ten feet high, waving their adieus to {{ him(Alleyne) }}. {{ He(Alleyne) }} waved back to them, and then hastened on, the lighter of heart for having fallen in with these strange purveyors of pleasure.
{{Alleyne}} had gone no great distance for all the many small passages that had befallen {{ him(Alleyne) }}. Yet to {{ him(Alleyne) }}, used as {{ he(Alleyne) }} was to a life of such quiet that the failure of a brewing or the altering of an anthem had seemed to be of the deepest import, the quick changing play of the lights and shadows of life was strangely startling and interesting. A gulf seemed to divide this brisk uncertain existence from the old steady round of work and of prayer which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had left behind {{ him(Alleyne) }}. The few hours that had passed since {{ he(Alleyne) }} saw the Abbey tower stretched out in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} memory until they outgrew whole months of the stagnant life of the cloister. As {{ he(Alleyne) }} walked and munched the soft bread from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} scrip, it seemed strange to {{ him(Alleyne) }} to feel that it was still warm from the ovens of Beaulieu.
When {{ he(Alleyne) }} passed Penerley, where were three cottages and a barn, {{ he(Alleyne) }} reached the edge of the tree country, and found the great barren heath of Blackdown stretching in front of {{ him(Alleyne) }}, all pink with heather and bronzed with the fading ferns. On the left the woods were still thick, but the road edged away from them and wound over the open. The sun lay low in the west upon a purple cloud, whence it threw a mild, chastening light over the wild moorland and glittered on the fringe of forest turning the withered leaves into flakes of dead gold, the brighter for the black depths behind them. To the seeing eye decay is as fair as growth, and death as life. The thought stole into {{Alleyne}}’s heart as {{ he(Alleyne) }} looked upon the autumnal country side and marvelled at its beauty. {{ He(Alleyne) }} had little time to dwell upon it however, for there were still six good miles between {{ him(Alleyne) }} and the nearest inn. {{ He(Alleyne) }} sat down by the roadside to partake of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} bread and cheese, and then with a lighter scrip {{ he(Alleyne) }} hastened upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} way.
There appeared to be more wayfarers on the down than in the forest. First {{ he(Alleyne) }} passed two Dominicans in their long black dresses, who swept by {{ him(Alleyne) }} with downcast looks and pattering lips, without so much as a glance at {{ him(Alleyne) }}. The clerk having made answer that {{ he(Alleyne) }} had heard the eels of Sowley well spoken of, the friar sucked in  his lips and hurried forward. Close at his heels came three laborers walking abreast, with spade and mattock over their shoulders. They sang some rude chorus right tunefully as they walked, but their English was so coarse and rough that to the ears of a cloister-bred {{ man(Alleyne) }} it sounded like a foreign and barbarous tongue. One of them carried a young bittern which they had caught upon the moor, and they offered it to {{Alleyne}} for a silver groat. Very glad {{ he(Alleyne) }} was to get safely past them, for, with their bristling red beards and their fierce blue eyes, they were uneasy men to bargain with upon a lonely moor.
Yet it is not always the burliest and the wildest who are the most to be dreaded. The workers looked hungrily at {{ him(Alleyne) }}, and then jogged onwards upon their way in slow, lumbering Saxon style. A worse {{ man(cripple) }} to deal with was a wooden-legged cripple who came hobbling down the path, so weak and so old to all appearance that a child need not stand in fear of {{ him(cripple) }}. So horrid was the causeless rage of the crooked creature, that the clerk came over a cold thrill, and took to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} heels until {{ he(Alleyne) }} was out of shot from stone or word. It seemed to {{ him(Alleyne) }} that in this country of England there was no protection for a {{ man(Alleyne) }} save that which lay in the strength of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} own arm and the speed of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} own foot. In the cloisters {{ he(Alleyne) }} had heard vague talk of the law—the mighty law which was higher than prelate or baron, yet no sign could {{ he(Alleyne) }} see of it. What was the benefit of a law written fair upon parchment, {{ he(Alleyne) }} wondered, if there were no officers to enforce it. As it fell out, however, {{ he(Alleyne) }} had that very evening, ere the sun had set, a chance of seeing how stern was the grip of the English law when it did happen to seize the offender.
A mile or so out upon the moor the road takes a very sudden dip into a hollow, with a peat-colored stream running swiftly down the centre of it. To the right of this stood, and stands to this day, an ancient barrow, or burying mound, covered deeply in a bristle of heather and bracken. {{Alleyne}} was plodding down the slope upon one side, when {{ he(Alleyne) }} saw an old dame coming towards {{ him(Alleyne) }} upon the other, limping with weariness and leaning heavily upon a stick. Where the path ran down a great stone had been fixed in the centre of the brook, but it was too far from the bank for her aged and uncertain feet. Twice she thrust forward at it, and twice she drew back, until at last, giving up in despair, she sat herself down by the brink and wrung her hands wearily. There she still sat when {{Alleyne}} reached the crossing.
“Come, mother quoth {{ he(Alleyne) }}, “it is not so very perilous a passage.”

“At Lyndenhurst; but alas! my money is at an end, and I could but get a dish of bran-porridge from the nunnery. “It is a long road yet to Brockenhurst,” said {{Alleyne}}; “but here is such bread and cheese as I have left, and here, too, is a penny which may help you to supper. May God be with you!”

{{ He(Alleyne) }} was moving away {{ himself(Alleyne) }}, when {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} eyes lit upon a strange sight, and one which sent a tingling through {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} skin. The negro had burrowed down into {{ hisd(negro) }} hiding-place upon the barrow, where {{ he(negro) }} might have lain snug enough, had it not been for the red gear upon {{ hisd(negro) }} head. In this manner, not only shall my own eternal salvation be secured, but thine also, for I shall never cease to intercede for thee.”
At the command of the bailiff they plucked off the {{ fellow(robber) }}’s shoe, and there sure enough at the side of the instep, wrapped in a piece of fine sendall, lay a long, dark splinter of wood. {{Alleyne}} gazed upon the scene—the portly velvet-clad official, the knot of hard-faced archers with their hands to the bridles of their horses, the thief with {{ hisd(robber) }} arms trussed back and {{ hisd(robber) }} doublet turned down upon {{ hisd(robber) }} shoulders. A minute later the bailiff and four of {{ hisd(bailiff) }} guards rode past {{ him(Alleyne) }} on their journey back to Southampton, the other two having been chosen as grave-diggers. As they passed, {{Alleyne}} saw that one of them was wiping her sword-blade upon the mane of her horse. A deadly sickness came over {{ him(Alleyne) }} at the sight, and sitting down by the wayside {{ he(Alleyne) }} burst out weeping, with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} nerves all in a jangle. It was a terrible world thought {{ he(Alleyne) }}, and it was hard to know which were the most to be dreaded, the criminals or the upholders of the law.
<h2>Chapter V How a strange company gathered at the “Pied Merlin.”  </h2>
The night had already fallen, and the moon was shining between the rifts of ragged, drifting clouds, before {{Alleyne}} Edric{{ son(Alleyne) }}, footsore and weary from the unwonted exercise, found {{ himself(Alleyne) }} in front of the forest inn which stood upon the outskirts of Lyndhurst. The building was long and low, standing back a little from the road, with two flambeaux blazing on either side of the door as a welcome to the traveller. From one window there thrust forth a long pole with a bunch of greenery tied to the end of it—a sign that liquor was to be sold within. As {{Alleyne}} walked up to it {{ he(Alleyne) }} perceived that it was rudely fashioned out of beams of wood, with twinkling lights all over where the glow from within shone through the chinks. The roof was poor and thatched; but in strange contrast to it there ran all along under the eaves a line of wooden shields, most gorgeously painted with chevron, bend, and saltire, and every heraldic device. By the door a horse stood tethered, the ruddy glow beating strongly upon his brown head and patient eyes, while his body stood back in the shadow.
{{Alleyne}} stood still in the roadway for a few minutes reflecting upon what {{ he(Alleyne) }} should do. It was, {{ he(Alleyne) }} knew, only a few miles further to Minstead, where {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ brother(Simon) }} dwelt. On the other hand, {{ he(Alleyne) }} had never seen this {{ brother(Simon) }} since childhood, and the reports which had come to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} ears concerning {{ him(Simon) }} were seldom to {{ hisd(Simon) }} advantage. By all accounts {{ he(Simon) }} was a hard and a bitter {{ man(Simon) }}.

It might be an evil start to come to {{ hisd(Simon) }} door so late and claim the shelter of {{ hisd(Simon) }} roof. Better to sleep here at this inn, and then travel on to Minstead in the morning. If {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ brother(Simon) }} would take {{ him(Alleyne) }} in, well and good. {{ He(Alleyne) }} would bide with {{ him(Simon) }} for a time and do what {{ he(Alleyne) }} might to serve {{ him(Simon) }}. If, on the other hand, {{ he(Simon) }} should have hardened {{ hisd(Simon) }} heart against {{ him(Alleyne) }}, {{ he(Alleyne) }} could only go on {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} way and do the best {{ he(Alleyne) }} might by {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} skill as a craftsman and a scrivener. At the end of a year {{ he(Alleyne) }} would be free to return to the cloisters, for such had been {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ father(Edric) }}’s bequest. A monkish upbringing, one year in the world after the age of twenty, and then a free selection one way or the other—it was a strange course which had been marked out for {{ him(Alleyne) }}. Such as it was, however, {{ he(perAlleyneson) }} had no choice but to follow it, and if {{ he(Alleyne) }} were to begin by making a friend of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ brother(Simon) }} {{ he(Alleyne) }} had best wait until morning before {{ he(Alleyne) }} knocked at {{ hisd(Simon) }} dwelling.
The rude plank door was ajar, but as {{Alleyne}} approached it there came from within such a gust of rough laughter and clatter of tongues that {{ he(Alleyne) }} stood irresolute upon the threshold. Summoning courage, however, and reflecting that it was a public dwelling, in which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had as much right as any other {{ man(Alleyne) }}, {{ he(Alleyne) }} pushed it open and stepped into the common room.
Though it was an autumn evening and somewhat warm, a huge fire of heaped billets of wood crackled and sparkled in a broad, open grate, some of the smoke escaping up a rude chimney, but the greater part rolling out into the room, so that the air was thick with it, and one coming from without could scarce catch one’s breath. On this fire a great cauldron bubbled and simmered, giving forth a rich and promising smell. Seated round it were a dozen or so folk, of all ages and conditions, who set up such a shout as {{Alleyne}} entered that {{ he(Alleyne) }} stood peering at them through the smoke, uncertain what this riotous greeting might portend.
“A rouse! A rouse!” cried one rough looking fellow in a tattered jerkin. “One more round of mead or ale and the score to the last comer.”
“‘Tis the law of the ‘Pied Merlin,’” shouted another. “Ho there, Dame Eliza! Here is fresh custom come to the house, and not a drain for the company.”
“I will take your orders, gentles; I will assuredly take your orders,” the landlady answered, bustling in with her hands full of leathern drinking-cups. “What is it that you drink, then? Beer for the lads and lasses of the forest, mead for the gleeman, strong waters for the tinker, and wine for the rest. It is an old custom of the house, young {{ ssir(Alleyne) }}. It has been the use at the ‘Pied Merlin’ this many a year back that the company should drink to the health of the last comer. Is it your pleasure to humor it?”
“Why, good dame” said {{Alleyne}}, “I would not offend the customs of your house, but it is only sooth when I say that my purse is a thin one. As far as two pence will go, however, I shall be right glad to do my part.”
“Plainly said and bravely spoken, my suckling,” roared a deep voice, and a heavy hand fell upon {{Alleyne}}’s shoulder. Looking up, {{ he(Alleyne) }} saw beside {{ him(Alleyne) }} {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} former cloister companion the renegade {{ monk(John) }}, Hordle {{John}}.
“By the thorn of Glastonbury! ill days are coming upon Beaulieu,” said {{ he(John) }}. “Here they have got rid in one day of the only two within their walls—for I have had mine eyes upon thee, youngster, and I know that for all thy baby-face there is the making of a {{ man(Alleyne) }} in thee. Then there is the {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }}, too. I am no friend of {{ his(Berghersh) }}, nor {{ he(Berghersh) }} of mine; but {{ he(Berghersh) }} has warm blood in {{ hisd(Berghersh) }} veins. {{ He(Berghersh) }} is the only grown {{ man(Berghersh) }} left among them. The others, what are they?”
“They are holy {{ men(Beaulieu) }},” {{Alleyne}} answered gravely.
“Holy {{ men(Beaulieu) }}? Holy cabbages! Holy bean-pods! What do they do but live and suck in sustenance and grow fat? If that be holiness, I could show you hogs in this forest who are fit to head the calendar. Think you it was for such a life that this good arm was fixed upon my shoulder, or that head placed upon your neck? There is work in the world, {{ man(Alleyne) }}, and it is not by hiding behind stone walls that we shall do it.”
“Why, then, did you join Beaulieu?” asked {{Alleyne}}.
“A fair enough question; but it is as fairly answered. I joined them because Margery Alspaye, of Bolder, married Crooked Thomas of Ringwood, and left a certain {{John}} of Hordle in the cold, for that {{ he(John) }} was a ranting, roving blade who was not to be trusted in wedlock. That was why, being fond and hot-headed, I left the world; and that is why, having had time to take thought, I am right glad to find myself back in it once more. Ill betide the day that ever I took off my yeoman’s {{ jerkin(John) }} to put on the white gown!”
Whilst {{ he(John) }} was speaking the landlady came in again, bearing a broad platter, upon which stood all the beakers and flagons charged to the brim with the brown ale or the ruby wine. Behind her came a maid with a high pile of wooden plates, and a great sheaf of spoons, one of which she handed round to each of the travellers. Two of the company, who were dressed in the weather-stained green doublet of foresters, lifted the big pot off the fire, and a third, with a huge pewter ladle, served out a portion of steaming collops to each guest. {{Alleyne}} bore {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} share and {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} ale-mug away with {{ him(Alleyne) }} to a retired trestle in the corner, where {{ he(Alleyne) }} could sup in peace and watch the strange scene, which was so different to those silent and well-ordered meals to which {{ he(Alleyne) }} was accustomed.
The room was not unlike a stable. The low ceiling, smoke-blackened and dingy, was pierced by several square trap-doors with rough-hewn ladders leading up to them. The walls of bare unpainted planks were studded here and there with great wooden pins, placed at irregular intervals and heights, from which hung over-tunics, wallets, whips, bridles, and saddles. Over the fireplace were suspended six or seven shields of wood, with coats-of-arms rudely daubed upon them, which showed by their varying degrees of smokiness and dirt that they had been placed there at different periods. There was no furniture, save a single long dresser covered with coarse crockery, and a number of wooden benches and trestles, the legs of which sank deeply into the soft clay floor, while the only light, save that of the fire, was furnished by three torches stuck in sockets on the wall, which flickered and crackled, giving forth a strong resinous odor. All this was novel and strange to the cloister-bred {{ youth(Alleyne) }}; but most interesting of all was the motley circle of guests who sat eating their collops round the blaze. They were a humble group of wayfarers, such as might have been found that night in any inn through the length and breadth of England; but to {{ him(Alleyne) }} they represented that vague world against which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had been so frequently and so earnestly warned. It did not seem to {{ him(Alleyne) }} from what {{ he(Alleyne) }} could see of it to be such a very wicked place after all.
Three or four of the men and women round the fire were evidently underkeepers and verderers from the forest, sunburned and unkempt, with the quick restless eye and lithe movements of the deer among which they lived. Close to the corner of the chimney sat a middle-aged gleeman, clad in a faded garb of Norwich cloth, the tunic of which was so outgrown that it did not fasten at the neck and at the waist. {{ Hisd(Will) }} face was swollen and coarse, and {{ hisd(Will) }} watery protruding eyes spoke of a life which never wandered very far from the wine-pot. A gilt harp, blotched with many stains and with two of its strings missing, was tucked under one of {{ hisd(Will) }} arms, while with the other {{ he(Will) }} scooped greedily at {{ hisd(Will) }} platter.
Next to {{ him(Will) }} sat two others of about the same age, one with a trimming of fur to {{ hisd(physician) }} coat, which gave {{ him(physician) }} a dignity which was evidently dearer to {{ him(physician) }} than {{ hisd(physician) }} comfort, for {{ he(physician) }} still drew it round {{ him(physician) }} in spite of the hot glare of the faggots. The other, clad in a dirty russet {{ suit(dentist) }} with a long sweeping doublet, had a cunning, foxy face with keen, twinkling eyes and a peaky {{ beard(dentist) }}. Next to {{ him(dentist) }} sat Hordle {{John}}, and beside {{ him(John) }} three other rough unkempt fellows with tangled beards and matted hair—free laborers from the adjoining farms, where small patches of freehold property had been suffered to remain scattered about in the heart of the royal demesne. The company was completed by a peasant in a rude dress of undyed sheepskin, with the old-fashioned galligaskins about {{ herd(sheepskins) }} legs, and a gayly dressed young {{ man(Cambrig) }} with striped cloak jagged at the edges and parti-colored hosen, who looked about {{ him(Cambrig) }} with high disdain upon {{ hisd(Cambrig) }} face, and held a blue smelling-flask to {{ hisd(Cambrig) }} nose with one hand, while {{ he(Cambrig) }} brandished a busy spoon with the other. In the corner a very fat {{ man(Wat) }} was lying all a-sprawl upon a truss, snoring stertorously, and evidently in the last stage of drunkenness.
“That is Wat the limner,” quoth the landlady, sitting down beside {{Alleyne}}, and pointing with the ladle to the sleeping {{ man(Wat) }}. “That is {{ he(Wat) }} who paints the signs and the tokens. Alack and alas that ever I should have been fool enough to trust {{ him(Wat) }}! Now, young {{ man(Alleyne) }}, what manner of a bird would you suppose a pied merlin to be—that being the proper sign of my hostel?”
“Why,” said {{Alleyne}}, “a merlin is a bird of the same form as an eagle or a falcon. I can well remember that learned {{ brother(Bartholomew) }} {{Bartholomew}}, who is deep in all the secrets of nature, pointed one out to me as we walked together near Vinney Ridge.”
“A falcon or an eagle, quotha? And pied, that is of two several colors. So anyone would say except this barrel of lies. {{ He(Wat) }} came to me, look you, saying that if I would furnish {{ him(Wat) }} with a gallon of ale, wherewith to strengthen {{ himself(Wat) }} as {{ he(Wat) }} worked, and also the pigments and a board, {{ he(Wat) }} would paint for me a noble pied merlin which I might hang along with the blazonry over my door. I, poor simple fool, gave {{ him(Wat) }} the ale and all that {{ he(Wat) }} craved, leaving {{ him(Wat) }} alone too, because {{ he(Wat) }} said that a {{ man(Wat) }}’s mind must be left untroubled when {{ he(Wat) }} had great work to do. When I came back the gallon jar was empty, and {{ he(Wat) }} lay as you see {{ him(Wat) }}, with the board in front of {{ him(Wat) }} with this sorry device.” She raised up a panel which was leaning against the wall, and showed a rude painting of a scraggy and angular fowl, with very long legs and a spotted body.
“Was that,” she asked, “like the bird which thou hast seen?”
{{Alleyne}} shook {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} head, smiling.
“No, nor any other bird that ever wagged a feather. It is most like a plucked pullet which has died of the spotted fever. And scarlet too! What would the gentles Dame Nicole Boarhunte, or Sir Bernard Brocas, of Roche Court, say if they saw such a thing—or, perhaps, even the {{ King(Edward) }}'s own Majesty {{ himself(Edwards) }} who often has ridden past this way, and who loves {{ hisd(Edwards) }} falcons as {{ he(Edwards) }} loves {{ hisd(Edwards) }} children? It would be the downfall of my house.”
“The matter is not past mending,” said {{Alleyne}}. “I pray you, good dame, to give me those three pigment-pots and the brush, and I shall try whether I cannot better this painting.”
Dame Eliza looked doubtfully at {{ him(Alleyne) }}, as though fearing some other stratagem, but, as {{ he(Alleyne) }} made no demand for ale, she finally brought the paints, and watched {{ him(Alleyne) }} as {{ he(Alleyne) }} smeared on {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} background, talking the while about the folk round the fire.
“The four forest lads and lasses must be jogging soon,” she said. “They bide at Emery Down, a mile or more from here. Yeomen prickers they are, who tend to the {{ King(Edward) }}'s hunt. The glee{{ man(Will) }} is called Floyting {{Will}}. {{ He(Will) }} comes from the north country, but for many years {{ he(Will) }} hath gone the round of the forest from Southampton to Christchurch. {{ He(Will) }} drinks much and pays little but it would make your ribs crackle to hear {{ him(Will) }} sing the ‘Jest of Hendy Tobias.’ Mayhap {{ he(Will) }} will sing it when the ale has warmed {{ him(Will) }}.”
“Who are those next to {{ him(Will) }}?” asked {{Alleyne}}, much interested. “{{ He(physician) }} of the fur mantle has a wise and reverent face.”
