“And what other nations have you seen in your travels, good {{ sirm(Samkin) }}?” asked {{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }}. {{ Hisd(Alleyne) }} young mind hungered for plain facts of life, after the long course of speculation and of mysticism on which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had been trained.
“I have seen the low countryman in arms, and I have nought to say against them. Heavy and slow are they by nature, and is not to be brought into battle for the sake of a flirt’s eyelash or the twang of a minstrel’s string, like the hotter blood of the south. But ma foi! lay hand on his wool-bales, or trifle with her velvet of Bruges, and out buzzes every stout burgher, like bees from the tee-hole, ready to lay on as though it were their one business in life. By our lady! they have shown the French at Courtrai and elsewhere that they are as deft in wielding steel as in welding it.”
“And the folk of Spain?”
“They too are very hardy soldiers, the more so as for many hundred years they have had to fight hard against the cursed followers of the black Mahound, who have pressed upon them from the south, and still, as I understand, hold the fairer half of the country. I had a turn with them upon the sea when they came over to Winchelsea and the good queen with her ladies sat upon the cliffs looking down at us, as if it had been joust or tourney. By my hilt! it was a sight that was worth the seeing, for all that was best in England was out on the water that day. We went forth in little ships and came back in great galleys—for of fifty tall ships of Spain, over two score flew the Cross of St. George ere the sun had set. But now, {{ youngster(Alleyne) }}, I have answered you freely, and I trow it is time that you answered me. Let things be plat and plain between us. I am a {{ man(Samkin) }} who shoots straight at {{ hisd(Samkin) }} mark. You saw the things I had with me at yonder hostel: name which you will, save only the box of rose-colored sugar which I take to the {{ Lady(Mary) }} Loring, and you shall have it if you will but come with me to France.”
“Nay,” said {{Alleyne}}, “I would gladly come with ye to France or where else ye will, just to list to your talk, and because ye are the only two friends that I have in the whole wide world outside of the cloisters; but, indeed, it may not be, for my duty is towards my {{ brother(Simon) }}, seeing that father and mother are dead, and {{ he(Simon) }} my elder. Besides, when ye talk of taking me to France, ye do not conceive how useless I should be to you, seeing that neither by training nor by nature am I fitted for the wars, and there seems to be nought but strife in those parts.”
“That comes from my fool’s talk,” cried the archer; “for being a {{ man(Samkin) }} of no learning myself, my tongue turns to blades and targets, even as my hand does. Know then that for every parchment in England there are twenty in France. For every statue, cut gem, shrine, carven screen, or what else might please the eye of a learned clerk, there are a good hundred to our one. At the spoiling of Carcasonne I have seen chambers stored with writing, though not one in our Company could read them. Again, in Arles and Nimes, and other towns that I could name, there are the great arches and fortalices still standing which were built of old by giants who came from the south. Can I not see by your brightened eye how you would love to look upon these things? Come then with me, and, by these ten finger-bones! there is not one of them which you shall not see.”
“I should indeed love to look upon them,” {{Alleyne}} answered; “but I have come from Beaulieu for a purpose, and I must be true to my service, even as thou art true to thine.”
“Bethink you again, mon ami,” quoth Aylward, “that you might do much good yonder, since there are three hundred in the Company, and none who has ever a word of grace for them, and yet the Virgin knows that there was never a set of soldiers who were in more need of it. Sickerly the one duty may balance the other. Your {{ brother(Simon) }} hath done without you this many a year, and, as I gather, {{ he(Simon) }} hath never walked as far as Beaulieu to see you during all that time, so {{ he(Simon) }} cannot be in any great need of you.”
“Besides,” said {{John}}, “the Socman of Minstead is a by-word through the forest, from Bramshaw Hill to Holmesley Walk. {{ He(Simon) }} is a drunken, brawling, perilous churl, as you may find to your cost.”
“The more reason that I should strive to mend {{ him(Simon) }},” quoth {{Alleyne}}. “There is no need to urge me, friends, for my own wishes would draw me to France, and it would be a joy to me if I could go with you. But indeed and indeed it cannot be, so here I take my leave of you, for yonder square tower amongst the trees upon the right must surely be the church of Minstead, and I may reach it by this path through the woods.”
“Well, God be with thee, {{ lad(Alleyne) }}!” cried the archer, pressing {{Alleyne}} to {{ hisd(Samkin) }} heart. “I am quick to love, and quick to hate and ‘fore God I am loth to part.”
“Would it not be well,” said {{John}}, “that we should wait here, and see what manner of greeting you have from your {{ brother(Simon) }}. You may prove to be as welcome as the {{ king(Edward) }}'s purveyor to the village dame.”
“Nay, nay,” {{ he(Alleyne) }} answered; “ye must not bide for me, for where I go I stay.”
“Yet it may be as well that you should know whither we go,” said the archer. “We shall now journey south through the woods until we come out upon the Christchurch road, and so onwards, hoping to-night to reach the castle of Sir William Montacute, Earl of Salisbury, of which {{ Sirr({{Nigel}} ) }} {{Nigel}} Loring is constable. There we shall bide, and it is like enough that for a month or more you may find us there, ere we are ready for our viage back to France.”
It was hard indeed for {{Alleyne}} to break away from these two new but hearty friends, and so strong was the combat between {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} conscience and {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} inclinations that {{ he(Alleyne) }} dared not look round, lest {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} resolution should slip away from {{ him(Alleyne) }}. It was not until {{ he(Alleyne) }} was deep among the tree trunks that {{ he(Alleyne) }} cast a glance backwards, when {{ he(Alleyne) }} found that {{ he(Alleyne) }} could still see them through the branches on the road above {{ him(Alleyne) }}. The archer was standing with folded arms, {{ hisd(Samkin) }} bow jutting from over {{ hisd(Samkin) }} shoulder, and the sun gleaming brightly upon {{ hisd(Samkin) }} head-piece and the links of {{ hisd(Samkin) }} chain-mail. Beside {{ him(Samkin) }} stood {{ hisd(Samkin) }} giant recruit, still clad in the home-spun and ill-fitting garments of the fuller of Lymington, with arms and legs shooting out of {{ hisd(John) }} scanty garb. Even as {{Alleyne}} watched them they turned upon their heels and plodded off together upon their way.
<h2>Chapter IX How Strange Things Befell in Minstead Wood</h2>
The path which the young clerk had now to follow lay through a magnificent forest of the very heaviest timber, where the giant bowls of oak and of beech formed long aisles in every direction, shooting up their huge branches to build the majestic arches of Nature’s own cathedral. Beneath lay a broad carpet of the softest and greenest moss, flecked over with fallen leaves, but yielding pleasantly to the foot of the traveller. The track which guided {{ him(Alleyne) }} was one so seldom used that in places it lost itself entirely among the grass, to reappear as a reddish rut between the distant tree trunks. It was very still here in the heart of the woodlands. The gentle rustle of the branches and the distant cooing of pigeons were the only sounds which broke in upon the silence, save that once {{Alleyne}} heard afar off a merry call upon a hunting bugle and the shrill yapping of the hounds.
It was not without some emotion that {{ he(Alleyne) }} looked upon the scene around {{ him(Alleyne) }}, for, in spite of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} secluded life, {{ he(Alleyne) }} knew enough of the ancient greatness of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} own family to be aware that the time had been when they had held undisputed and paramount sway over all that tract of country. {{ Hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ father(Edric) }} could trace {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} pure Saxon lineage back to that Winifred Malf who had held the manors of Bisterne and of Minstead at the time when the Norman first set mailed foot upon English soil. The afforestation of the district, however, and its conversion into a royal demesne had clipped off a large section of her estate, while other parts had been confiscated as a punishment for her supposed complicity in an abortive Saxon rising. The fate of the ancestor had been typical of that of her descendants. During three hundred years their domains had gradually contracted, sometimes through royal or feudal encroachment, and sometimes through such gifts to the Church as that with which {{Alleyne}}’s {{ father(Edric) }} had opened the doors of Beaulieu Abbey to {{ hisd(Edric) }} younger {{ son(Alleyne) }}. The importance of the family had thus dwindled, but they still retained the old Saxon manor-house, with a couple of farms and a grove large enough to afford pannage to a hundred pigs—“sylva de centum porcis,” as the old family parchments describe it. Above all, the owner of the soil could still hold {{ hisd(Simon) }} head high as the veritable Socman of Minstead—that is, as holding the land in free socage, with no feudal superior, and answerable to no one lower than the{{ king(Edward) }}. Knowing this, {{Alleyne}} felt some little glow of worldly pride as {{ he(Alleyne) }} looked for the first time upon the land with which so many generations of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} ancestors had been associated. {{ He(Alleyne) }} pushed on the quicker, twirling {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} staff merrily, and looking out at every turn of the path for some sign of the old Saxon residence.
{{ He(Alleyne) }} was suddenly arrested, however, by the appearance of a wild-looking {{ fellow(sheepskins) }} armed with a club, who sprang out from behind a tree and barred {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} passage. {{ He(sheepskins) }} was a rough, powerful peasant, with cap and tunic of untanned sheepskin, leather breeches, and galligaskins round legs and feet.
“Stand!” {{ he(sheepskins) }} shouted, raising {{ hisd(sheepskins) }} heavy cudgel to enforce the order. “Who are you who walk so freely through the wood? Whither would you go, and what is your errand?”
“Why should I answer your questions, my friend?” said {{Alleyne}}, standing on {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} guard.
“Because your tongue may save your pate. But where have I looked upon your face before?”
“No longer ago than last night at the ‘Pied Merlin,’” the clerk answered, recognizing the escaped serf who had been so outspoken as to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} wrongs.
“By the Virgin! yes. You were the little clerk who sat so mum in the corner, and then cried fy on the glee{{ man(Will) }}. What hast in the scrip?”
“Naught of any price.”
“How can I tell that, clerk? Let me see.”
“Not I.”
“Fool! I could pull you limb from limb like a pullet. What would you have? Hast forgot that we are alone far from all? How can your clerkship help you? Wouldst lose scrip and life too?”
“I will part with neither without fight.”
“A fight, quotha? A fight betwixt spurred cock and new hatched chicken! Thy fighting days may soon be over.”
“Hadst asked me in the name of charity I would have given freely,” cried {{Alleyne}}. “As it stands, not one farthing shall you have with my free will, and when I see my {{ brother(Simon) }}, the Socman of Minstead, {{ he(Simon) }} will raise hue and cry from vill to vill, from hundred to hundred, until you are taken as a common robber and a scourge to the country.”
The outlaw sank {{ hisd(sheepskins) }} club. “The Socman’s {{ brother(Alleyne) }}!” {{ he(sheepskins) }} gasped. “Now, by the keys of Peter! I had rather that hand withered and tongue was palsied ere I had struck or miscalled you. If you are the Socman’s {{ brother(Alleyne) }} you are one of the right side, I warrant, for all your clerkly dress.”
“His {{ brother(Alleyne) }} I am,” said {{Alleyne}}. “But if I were not, is that reason why you should molest me on the {{ king(Edward) }}'s ground?”
“I give not the pip of an apple for {{ king(Edward) }} or for noble,” cried the serf passionately. “Ill have I had from them, and ill I shall repay them. I am a good friend to my friends, and, by the Virgin! an evil foeman to my foes.”
“And therefore the worst of foemen to thyself,” said {{Alleyne}}. “But I pray you, since you seem to know {{ him(Simon) }}, to point out to me the shortest path to my {{ brother(Simon) }}’s house.”
The serf was about to reply, when the clear ringing call of a bugle burst from the wood close behind them, and {{Alleyne}} caught sight for an instant of the dun side and white breast of a lordly stag glancing swiftly betwixt the distant tree trunks. A minute later came the shaggy deer-hounds, a dozen or fourteen of them, running on a hot scent, with nose to earth and tail in air. As they streamed past the silent forest around broke suddenly into loud life, with galloping of hoofs, crackling of brushwood, and the short, sharp cries of the hunters. Close behind the pack rode a fourrier and a yeoman-pricker, whooping on the laggards and encouraging the leaders, in the shrill half-French jargon which was the language of venery and woodcraft. {{Alleyne}} was still gazing after them, listening to the loud “Hyke-a-Bayard! Hyke-a-Pomers! Hyke-a-Lebryt!” with which they called upon their favorite hounds, when a group of horsemen crashed out through the underwood at the very spot where the serf and {{ he(Alleyne) }} were standing.
The one who led was a {{ man(Edward) }} between fifty and sixty years of age, war-worn and weather-beaten, with a broad, thoughtful forehead and eyes which shone brightly from under his fierce and overhung brows. {{ Hisd(Edward) }} {{ beard(Edward) }} streaked thickly with gray, and spoke of a passionate nature, while the long, finely cut face and firm mouth marked the leader of men and women  {{ Hisd(Edward) }} figure was erect and soldierly, and {{ he(Edward) }} rode {{ hisd(Edward) }} horse with the careless grace of a {{ man(Edward) }} whose life had been spent in the saddle. In common garb, {{ hisd(Edward) }} masterful face and flashing eye would have marked {{ him(Edward) }} as one who was born to rule; but now, with {{ hisd(Edward) }} silken tunic powdered with golden fleurs-de-lis, {{ hisd(Edward) }} velvet mantle lined with the royal minever, and the lions of England stamped in silver upon {{ hisd(Edward) }} harness, none could fail to recognize the noble {{Edward}}, most warlike and powerful of all the long line of fighting monarchs who had ruled the Anglo-Norman race. {{Alleyne}} doffed hat and bowed head at the sight of {{ him(Edward) }}, but the serf folded {{ hisd(sheepskins) }} hands and leaned them upon {{ hisd(sheepskins) }} cudgel, looking with little love at the knot of nobles and knights-in-waiting who rode behind the {{ king(Edward) }}.
“Ha!” cried {{Edward}}, reining up for an instant {{ hisd(Edward) }} powerful black steed. “Le cerf est passe? Non? Ici, Brocas; tu parles Anglais.”
“The deer, clowns?” said a hard-visaged, swarthy-faced {{ man(Brocas) }}, who rode at the {{ king(Edward) }}’s elbow. “If ye have headed it back it is as much as your ears are worth.”
“It passed by the blighted beech there,” said {{Alleyne}}, pointing, “and the hounds were hard at its heels.”
“It is well,” cried {{Edward}}, still speaking in French: for, though {{ he(Edward) }} could understand English, {{ he(Edward) }} had never learned to express {{ himself(Edward) }} in so barbarous and unpolished a tongue. “By my faith, sirs,” {{ he(Edward) }} continued, half turning in {{ hisd(Edward) }} saddle to address {{ hisd(Edward) }} escort, “unless my woodcraft is sadly at fault, it is a stag of six tines and the finest that we have roused this journey. A golden St. Hubert to the one who is the first to sound the mort.” {{ He(Edward) }} shook {{ hisd(Edward) }} bridle as {{ he(Edward) }} spoke, and thundered away, {{ hisd(Edward) }} knights lying low upon their horses and galloping as hard as whip and spur would drive them, in the hope of winning the {{ king(Edward) }}’s prize. Away they drove down the long green glade—bay horses, black and gray, riders clad in every shade of velvet, fur, or silk, with glint of brazen horn and flash of knife and spear. One only lingered, the black-browed {{ Baron(Brocas) }} Brocas, who, making a gambade which brought {{ him(Brocas) }} within arm-sweep of the serf, slashed {{ him(sheepskins) }} across the face with {{ hisd(Brocas) }} riding-whip. “Doff, dog, doff,” {{ he(Brocas) }} hissed, “when a monarch deigns to lower {{ hisd(Edward) }} eyes to such as you!”—then spurred through the underwood and was gone, with a gleam of steel shoes and flutter of dead leaves.
The villein took the cruel blow without wince or cry, as one to whom stripes are a birthright and an inheritance. {{ Hisd(sheepskins) }} eyes flashed, however, and {{ he(sheepskins) }} shook {{ hisd(sheepskins) }} bony hand with a fierce wild gesture after the retreating figure.
“Black hound of Gascony,” {{ he(sheepskins) }} muttered, “evil the day that you and those like you set foot in free England! I know thy kennel of Rochecourt. The night will come when I may do to thee and thine what you and your class have wrought upon mine and me. May God smite me if I fail to smite thee, thou French robber, with thy consort and thy child and all that is under thy castle roof!”
“Forbear!” cried {{Alleyne}}. “Mix not God’s name with these unhallowed threats! And yet it was a coward’s blow, and one to stir the blood and loose the tongue of the most peaceful. Let me find some soothing simples and lay them on the weal to draw the sting.”
“Nay, there is but one thing that can draw the sting, and that the future may bring to me. But, clerk, if you would see your {{ brother(Simon) }} you must on, for there is a meeting to-day, and {{ hisd(Simon) }} merry men will await {{ him(Simon) }} ere the shadows turn from west to east. I pray you not to hold {{ him(Simon) }} back, for it would be an evil thing if all the stout lads were there and the leader a-missing. I would come with you, but sooth to say I am stationed here and may not move. The path over yonder, betwixt the oak and the thorn, should bring you out into {{ hisd(Simon) }} nether field.”
{{Alleyne}} lost no time in following the directions of the wild, masterless {{ man(sheepskins) }}, whom {{ he(Alleyne) }} left among the trees where {{ he(Alleyne) }} had found {{ him(sheepskins) }}. {{ Hisd(Alleyne) }} heart was the heavier for the encounter, not only because all bitterness and wrath were abhorrent to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} gentle nature, but also because it disturbed {{ him(Alleyne) }} to hear {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ brother(Simon) }} spoken of as though {{ he(Simon) }} were a chief of outlaws or the leader of a party against the state. Indeed, of all the things which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had seen yet in the world to surprise {{ him(Alleyne) }} there was none more strange than the hate which class appeared to bear to class. The talk of laborer, woodman and villein in the inn had all pointed to the wide-spread mutiny, and now {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ brother(Simon) }}’s name was spoken as though {{ he(Simon) }} were the very centre of the universal discontent. In good truth, the commons throughout the length and breadth of the land were heart-weary of this fine game of chivalry which had been played so long at their expense. So long as knight and baron were a strength and a guard to the kingdom they might be endured, but now, when all knew that the great battles in France had been won by English yeomen and Welsh stabbers, warlike fame, the only fame to which one’s class had ever aspired, appeared to have deserted the plate-clad horsemen. The sports of the lists had done much in days gone by to impress the minds of the people, but the plumed and unwieldy champion was no longer an object either of fear or of reverence to those whose loved ones had shot into the press at Crecy or Poitiers, and seen the proudest chivalry in the world unable to make head against the weapons of disciplined peasants. Power had changed hands. The protector had become the protected, and the whole fabric of the feudal system was tottering to a fall. Hence the fierce mutterings of the lower classes and the constant discontent, breaking out into local tumult and outrage, and culminating some years later in the great rising of Tyler. What {{Alleyne}} saw and wondered at in Hampshire would have appealed equally to the traveller in any other English county from the Channel to the marches of Scotland.
{{ He(Alleyne) }} was following the track, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} misgivings increasing with every step which took {{ him(Alleyne) }} nearer to that home which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had never seen, when of a sudden the trees began to thin and the sward to spread out onto a broad, green lawn, where five cows lay in the sunshine and droves of black swine wandered unchecked. A brown forest stream swirled down the centre of this clearing, with a rude bridge flung across it, and on the other side was a second field sloping up to a long, low-lying wooden house, with thatched roof and open squares for windows. {{Alleyne}} gazed across at it with flushed cheeks and sparkling eyes—for this, {{ he(Alleyne) }} knew, must be the home of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} ancestors. In the yellow shimmer of the autumn sunshine it lay as peacefully and as still as {{ he(Alleyne) }} had oft pictured it to {{ himself(Alleyne) }} in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} dreams.

Good, quiet, uncomplaining mother Nature, long slighted and miscalled, still bides her time and draws to her bosom the most errant of her children.

<h2>Chapter X - How Hordle {{John}} Found a {{ Man(Nigel) }} Whom {{ He(John) }} Might Follow</h2>
If {{ he(Alleyne) }} might not return to Beaulieu within the year, and if {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ brother(Simon) }}’s dogs were to be set upon {{ him(Alleyne) }} if {{ he(Alleyne) }} showed face upon Minstead land, then indeed {{ he(Alleyne) }} was adrift upon earth. North, south, east, and west—he might turn where {{ he(Alleyne) }} would, but all was equally chill and cheerless. The afternoon was not very advanced, for all that had befallen {{ him(Alleyne) }}. When on is afoot at cock-crow much may be done in the day. It was no easy or light thing to journey through this great forest, which was some twenty miles from east to west and a good sixteen from Bramshaw Woods in the north to Lymington in the south. {{Alleyne}}, however, had the good fortune to fall in with a woodswoman, axe upon shoulder, trudging along in the very direction that {{ he(Alleyne) }} wished to go. It was the age of martial men and women. The deeds of black Agnes of Dunbar, of Lady Salisbury and of the Countess of Montfort, were still fresh in the public minds. With such examples before them the wives of the English captains had become as warlike as their mates, and ordered their castles in their absence with the prudence and discipline of veteran seneschals. Right easy were the Montacutes of their Castle of Twynham, and little had they to dread from roving galley or French squadron, while {{ Lady(Mary) }} {{Mary}} Loring had the ordering of it. Yet even in that age it was thought that, though a lady might have a soldier’s heart, it was scarce as well that she should have a soldier’s face. There were men and women who said that of all the stern passages and daring deeds by which {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} Loring had proved the true temper of {{ hisd(Nigel) }} courage, not the least was {{ hisd(Nigel) }} wooing and winning of so forbidding a {{ dame(Mary) }}.
“I tell you, my fair {{ lord(Nigel) }},” {{ she(Mary) }} was saying, “that it is no fit training for the {{ lady(Maude) }}: hawks and hounds, rotes and citoles singing a French rondel, or reading the Gestes de Doon de Mayence, as I found {{ her(Maude) }} yesternight, pretending sleep, the artful, with the corner of the scroll thrusting forth from under {{ herd(Maude) }} pillow. Lent {{ her(Maude) }} by {{ Father(Christopher) }} {{Christopher}} of the priory, forsooth—that is ever {{ herd(Maude) }} answer. How shall all this help {{ her(Maude) }} when {{ she(Maude) }} has castle of {{ herd(Maude) }} own to keep, with a hundred mouths all agape for beef and beer?”
“True, my sweet bird, true,” answered the knight, picking a comfit from {{ hisd(Nigel) }} gold drageoir. “The {{ maid(Maude) }} is like the young {{ filly(Maude) }}, which kicks heels and plunges for very lust of life. Give {{ her(Maude) }} time, {{ damee(Mary) }}, give {{ her(Maude) }} time.”
“Well, I know that my father would have given me, not time, but a good hazel-stick across my shoulders. Ma foi! I know not what the world is coming to, when young {{ maiden(Maude) }}s may flout their elders. I wonder that you do not correct {{ her(Maude) }}, my fair {{ lord(Nigel) }}.”
“Nay, my heart’s comfort, I never raised hand to an innocent {{ lady(Maude) }} yet, and it would be a passing strange thing if I began on my own flesh and blood. It was a {{ woman(Maude) }}’s hand which cast this lime into mine eyes, and though I saw {{ her(Maude) }} stoop, and might well have stopped {{ her(Maude) }} ere {{ she(Maude) }} threw, I deemed it unworthy of my knighthood to hinder or balk{{ her(Maude) }}.”
“The hussy!” cried {{ Lady(Mary) }} Loring clenching {{ herd(Mary) }} broad right hand. “I would I had been at the side of {{ her(Mary) }}!”
“And so would I, since you would have been the nearer me my own. But I doubt not that you are right, and that {{Maude}}’s wings need clipping, which I may leave in your hands when I am gone, for, in sooth, this peaceful life is not for me, and were it not for your gracious kindness and loving care I could not abide it a week. I hear that there is talk of warlike muster at Bordeaux once more, and by St. Paul! it would be a new thing if the lions of England and the red pile of Chandos were to be seen in the field, and the roses of Loring were not waving by their side.”
“Now woe worth me but I feared it!” cried {{ she(Mary) }}, with the color all struck from {{ herd(Mary) }} face. “I have noted your absent mind, your kindling eye, your trying and riveting of old harness. Consider my sweet {{ lord(Nigel) }}, that you have already won much honor, that we have seen but little of each other, that you bear upon your body the scar of over twenty wounds received in I know not how many bloody encounters. Have you not done enough for honor and the public cause?”
“My {{ lady(Mary) }}, when our liege {{ lord(Edward) }}, the {{ king(Edward) }}, at three score years, and my {{ Lord(Chandos) }} Chandos at three-score and ten, are blithe and ready to lay lance in rest for England’s cause, it would ill be-seem me to prate of service done. It is sooth that I have received seven and twenty wounds. There is the more reason that I should be thankful that I am still long of breath and sound in limb. I have also seen some bickering and scuffling. Six great land battles I count, with four upon sea, and seven and fifty onfalls, skirmishes and bushments. I have held two and twenty towns, and I have been at the intaking of thirty-one. Surely then it would be bitter shame to me, and also to you, since my fame is yours, that I should now hold back if a {{ man(Nigel) }}’s work is to be done. Besides, bethink you how low is our purse, with bailiff and reeve ever croaking of empty farms and wasting lands. Were it not for this constableship which the Earl of Salisbury hath bestowed upon us we could scarce uphold the state which is fitting to our degree. Therefore, my sweeting, there is the more need that I should turn to where there is good pay to be earned and brave ransoms to be won.”
“Ah, my dear {{ lord(Nigel) }},” quoth {{ she(Mary) }}, with sad, weary eyes. “I thought that at last I had you to mine own self, even though your youth had been spent afar from my side. Yet my voice, as I know well, should speed you on to glory and renown, not hold you back when fame is to be won. Yet what can I say, for all men and women know that your valor needs the curb and not the spur. It goes to my heart that you should ride forth now a mere knight bachelor, when there is no noble in the land who hath so good a claim to the square pennon, save only that you have not the money to uphold it.”
“And whose fault that, my sweet bird?” said {{ he(Nigel) }}.
“No fault, my fair {{ lord(Nigel) }}, but a virtue: for how many rich ransoms have you won, and yet have scattered the crowns among page and archer and varlet, until in a week you had not as much as would buy food and forage. It is a most knightly largesse, and yet withouten money how can one rise?”
“Dirt and dross!” cried {{ he(Nigel) }}.
“What matter rise or fall, so that duty be done and honor gained. Banneret or bachelor, square pennon or forked, I would not give a denier for the difference, and the less since {{ Sirr(Chandos) }} Chandos, chosen flower of English chivalry, is {{ himself(Chandos) }}  but a humble knight. But meanwhile fret not thyself, my heart’s dove, for it is like that there may be no war waged, and we must await the news. But here are three strangers, and one, as I take it, a soldier fresh from service. It is likely that {{ he(Chandos) }} may give us word of what is stirring over the water.”
{{ Lady(Mary) }} {{Mary}}, glancing up, saw in the fading light three companions walking abreast down the road, all gray with dust, and stained with travel, yet chattering merrily between themselves. {{ He(Alleyne) }} in the midst was young and comely, with youthful open face and bright gray eyes, which glanced from right to left as though {{ he(Alleyne) }} found the world around {{ him(Alleyne) }} both new and pleasing. To {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} right walked a huge red-headed {{ man(John) }}, with broad smile and merry twinkle, whose clothes seemed to be bursting and splitting at every seam, as though {{ he(John) }} were some lusty chick who was breaking bravely from {{ hisd(John) }} shell. On the other side, with {{ hisd(Samkin) }} knotted hand upon the young {{ man(Alleyne) }}’s shoulder, came a stout and burly archer, brown and fierce eyed, with sword at belt and long yellow yew-stave peeping over {{ hisd(Samkin) }} shoulder. Hard face, battered head piece, dinted brigandine, with faded red lion of St. George ramping on a discolored ground, all proclaimed as plainly as words that {{ he(Samkin) }} was indeed from the land of war. {{ He(Samkin) }} looked keenly at {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} as {{ he(Samkin) }} approached, and then, plunging {{ hisd(Samkin) }} hand under {{ hisd(Samkin) }} breastplate, {{ he(Samkin) }} stepped up to {{ him(Nigel) }} with a rough, uncouth bow to the {{ lady(Mary) }}.
“Your pardon, fair {{ sirm(Nigel) }},” said {{ he(Samkin) }}, “but I know you the moment I clap eyes on you, though in sooth I have seen you oftener in steel than in velvet. I have drawn string besides you at La Roche-d’Errien, Romorantin, Maupertuis, Nogent, Auray, and other places.”
“Then, good archer, I am right glad to welcome you to Twynham Castle, and in the steward’s room you will find provant for yourself and comrades. To me also your face is known, though mine eyes play such tricks with me that I can scarce be sure of my own squire. Rest awhile, and you shall come to the hall anon and tell us what is passing in France, for I have heard that it is likely that our pennons may flutter to the south of the great Spanish mountains ere another year be passed.”
“There was talk of it in Bordeaux,” answered the archer, “and I saw myself that the armorers and smiths were as busy as rats in a wheat-rick. But I bring you this letter from the valiant Gascon knight, {{ Sirr(Claude) }} {{Claude}} Latour. And to you, {{ Lady(Mary) }},” {{ he(Samkin) }} added after a pause, “I bring from {{ him(Claude) }} this box of red sugar of Narbonne, with every courteous and knightly greeting which a gallant cavalier may make to a fair and noble {{ damee(Mary) }}.”
This little speech had cost the blunt bow{{ man(Samkin) }} much pains and planning; but {{ he(Samkin) }} might have spared {{ hisd(Samkin) }} breath, for the {{ lady(Mary) }} was quite as much absorbed as {{ herd(Mary) }} {{ lord(Nigel) }} in the letter, which they held between them, a hand on either corner, spelling it out very slowly, with drawn brows and muttering lips. As they read it, {{Alleyne}}, who stood with Hordle {{John}} a few paces back from their comrade, saw the {{ lady(Mary) }} catch {{ herd(Mary) }} breath, while the knight laughed softly to {{ himself(Nigel) }}.
“You see, dear heart,” said {{ he(Nigel) }}, “that they will not leave the old dog in {{ hisd(Nigel) }} kennel when the game is afoot. And what of this White Company, archer?”
“Ah, {{ sirm(Nigel) }}, you speak of dogs,” cried Aylward; “but there are a pack of lusty hounds who are ready for any quarry, if they have but a good huntsman to halloo them on. {{ Sirm(Nigel) }}, we have been in the wars together, and I have seen many a brave following but never such a set of woodland boys as this. They do but want you at their head, and who will bar the way to them!”
“Pardieu!” said {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, “if they are all like their messenger, they are indeed men and women of whom a leader may be proud. Your name, good archer?”
“Samkin Aylward, {{ sirm(Nigel) }}, of the Hundred of Easebourne and the Rape of Chichester.”
“And this giant behind you?”
“He is big {{John}}, of Hordle, a forest {{ man(John) }}, who hath now taken service in the Company.”

{{ He(Nigel) }} pointed as {{ he(Nigel) }} spoke to a huge rough-hewn block which lay by the roadside, deep sunken from its own weight in the reddish earth. “I would fain to France with my friends here,” {{Alleyne}} answered; “but I am a {{ man(Alleyne) }} of peace—a reader, exorcist, acolyte, and clerk.”

Ma foi! there were those who wished that {{ he(Alleyne) }} would have less care for their souls and a little more for their bodies!”
“It is well to have a learned clerk in every troop,” said {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}. “By St. Paul, there are men and women so caitiff that they think more of a scrivener’s pen than of their lady’s smile, and do their devoir in hopes that they may fill a line in a chronicle or make a tag to a jongleur’s romance. I remember well that, at the siege of Retters, there was a little, sleek, fat clerk of the name of Chaucer, who was so apt at rondel, sirvente, or tonson, that no one dare give back a foot from the walls, lest one find it all set down in rhymes and sung by every underling and varlet in the camp. But, my soul’s bird, you hear me prate as though all were decided, when I have not yet taken counsel either with you or with my lady mother. Let us to the chamber, while these strangers find such fare as pantry and cellar may furnish.”
“The night air strikes chill,” said the {{ lady(Mary) }}, and turned down the road with {{ herd(Mary) }} hand upon {{ herd(Mary) }} {{ lord(Nigel) }}’s arm. The three comrades dropped behind and followed: Aylward much the lighter for having accomplished {{ hisd(Samkin) }} mission, {{Alleyne}} full of wonderment at the humble bearing of so renowned a captain, and {{John}} loud with snorts and sneers, which spoke {{ hisd(John) }} disappointment and contempt.
“What ails the {{ man(John) }}?” asked Aylward in surprise.
“I have been cozened and bejaped,” quoth {{ he(John) }} gruffly.
“By whom, {{ Sirr(John) }} Samson the strong?”
“By thee, {{ Sirr(Samkin) }} Balaam the false prophet.”
“By my hilt!” cried the archer, “though I be not Balaam, yet I hold converse with the very creature that spake to {{ him(Samkin) }}. What is amiss, then, and how have I played you false?”
“Why, marry, did you not say, and {{Alleyne}} here will be my witness, that, if I would hie to the wars with you, you would place me under a leader who was second to none in all England for valor? “Is that where the shoe galls?” cried the bow{{ man(Samkin) }}, and laughed aloud. “I will ask you what you think of {{ him(Nigel) }} three months hence, if we be all alive; for sure I am that——”
Aylward’s words were interrupted by an extraordinary hubbub which broke out that instant some little way down the street in the direction of the Priory. There was deep-mouthed shouting of men, frightened shrieks of women, howling and barking of curs, and over all a sullen, thunderous rumble, indescribably menacing and terrible. Round the corner of the narrow street there came rushing a brace of whining dogs with tails tucked under their legs, and after them a white-faced burgher, with outstretched hands and wide-spread fingers, his hair all abristle and his eyes glinting back from one shoulder to the other, as though some great terror were at his very heels.
“Fly, my {{ lady(Mary) }}, fly!” he screeched, and whizzed past them like bolt from bow; while close behind came lumbering a huge black bear, with red tongue lolling from hismouth, and a broken chain jangling behind him. To right and left the folk flew for arch and doorway. Hordle {{John}} caught up the {{ Lady(Mary) }} Loring as though {{ she(Mary) }} had been a feather, and sprang with {{ her(Mary) }} into an open porch; while Aylward, with a whirl of French oaths, plucked at {{ hisd(Samkin) }} quiver and tried to unsling {{ hisd(Samkin) }} bow. {{Alleyne}}, all unnerved at so strange and unwonted a sight, shrunk up against the wall with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} eyes fixed upon the frenzied creature, which came bounding along with ungainly speed, looking the larger in the uncertain light, its huge jaws agape, with blood and slaver trickling to the ground. {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} alone, unconscious to all appearance of the universal panic, walked with unfaltering step up the centre of the road, a silken handkerchief in one hand and {{ hisd(Nigel) }} gold comfit-box in the other. It sent the blood cold through {{Alleyne}}’s veins to see that as they came together—the {{ man(Nigel) }} and the beast—the creature reared up, with eyes ablaze with fear and hate, and whirled its great paws above the knight to smite {{ him(Nigel) }} to the earth. {{ He(Nigel) }}, however, blinking with puckered eyes, reached up {{ hisd(Nigel) }} kerchief, and flicked the beast twice across the snout with it. “Ah, saucy! saucy,” quoth {{ he(Nigel) }}, with gentle chiding; on which the bear, uncertain and puzzled, dropped its four legs to earth again, and, waddling back, was soon swathed in ropes by the bear-ward and a crowd of peasants who had been in close pursuit.
A scared woman was the keeper; for, having chained the brute to a stake while she drank a stoup of ale at the inn, it had been baited by stray curs, until, in wrath and madness, it had plucked loose the chain, and smitten or bitten all who came in its path. Most scared of all was she to find that the creature had come nigh to harm the nobles of the castle, who had power to place her in the stretch-neck or to have the skin scourged from her shoulders. Yet, when she came with bowed head and humble entreaty for forgiveness, she was met with a handful of small silver from {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, whose {{ lady(Mary) }}, however, was less charitably disposed, being much ruffled in {{ herd(Mary) }} dignity by the manner in which {{ she(Mary) }} had been hustled from {{ herd(Mary) }} {{ lord(Nigel) }}’s side.
As they passed through the castle gate, {{John}} plucked at Aylward’s sleeve, and the two fell behind.
“I must crave your pardon, comrade,” said {{ he(John) }}, bluntly. “I was a fool not to know that a little rooster may be the gamest. I believe that this {{ man(John) }} is indeed a leader whom we may follow.”
<h2>Chapter XI - How a Young Shepherd Had a Perilous Flock</h2>
Black was the mouth of Twynham Castle, though a pair of torches burning at the further end of the gateway cast a red glare over the outer bailey, and sent a dim, ruddy flicker through the rough-hewn arch, rising and falling with fitful brightness. Over the door the travellers could discern the escutcheon of the Montacutes, a roebuck gules on a field argent, flanked on either side by smaller shields which bore the red roses of the veteran constable. As they passed over the drawbridge, {{Alleyne}} marked the gleam of arms in the embrasures to right and left, and they had scarce set foot upon the causeway ere a hoarse blare burst from a bugle, and, with screech of hinge and clank of chain, the ponderous bridge swung up into the air, drawn by unseen hands. At the same instant the huge portcullis came rattling down from above, and shut off the last fading light of day. {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} and {{ hisd(Nigel) }} {{ lady(Mary) }} walked on in deep talk, while a fat under-steward took charge of the three comrades, and led them to the buttery, where beef, bread, and beer were kept ever in readiness for the wayfarer. After a hearty meal and a dip in the trough to wash the dust from them, they strolled forth into the bailey, where the bowman peered about through the darkness at wall and at keep, with the carping eyes of one who has seen something of sieges, and is not likely to be satisfied. To {{Alleyne}} and to {{John}}, however, it appeared to be as great and as stout a fortress as could be built by the hands of man or woman.
Erected by Dame Balwina de Redvers in the old fighting days of the twelfth century, when people thought much of war and little of comfort, Castle Twynham had been designed as a stronghold pure and simple, unlike those later and more magnificent structures where warlike strength had been combined with the magnificence of a palace. From the time of the Edwards such buildings as Conway or Caernarvon castles, to say nothing of Royal Windsor, had shown that it was possible to secure luxury in peace as well as security in times of trouble. {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}’s trust, however, still frowned above the smooth-flowing waters of the Avon, very much as the stern race of early Anglo-Normans had designed it. There were the broad outer and inner bailies, not paved, but sown with grass to nourish the sheep and cattle which might be driven in on sign of danger. All round were high and turreted walls, with at the corner a bare square-faced keep, gaunt and windowless, rearing up from a lofty mound, which made it almost inaccessible to an assailant. Against the bailey-walls were rows of frail wooden houses and leaning sheds, which gave shelter to the archers and men-at-arms who formed the garrison. The doors of these humble dwellings were mostly open, and against the yellow glare from within {{Alleyne}} could see the bearded fellows cleaning their harness, while their wives would come out for a gossip, with their needlework in their hands, and their long black shadows streaming across the yard. The air was full of the clack of their voices and the merry prattling of children, in strange contrast to the flash of arms and constant warlike challenge from the walls above.
“Methinks a company of school lads could hold this place against an army,” quoth {{John}}.
“And so say I,” said {{Alleyne}}.
“Nay, there you are wide of the clout,” the bow{{ man(Samkin) }} said gravely. “By my hilt! I have seen a stronger fortalice carried in a summer evening. I remember such a one in Picardy, with a name as long as a Gascon’s pedigree. It was when I served under {{ Sirr(Robert) }} {{Robert}} Knolles, before the days of the Company; and we came by good plunder at the sacking of it. I had myself a great silver bowl, with two goblets, and a plastron of Spanish steel. Pasques Dieu! there are some fine women over yonder! Mort de ma vie! see to that one in the doorway! I will go speak to her. But whom have we here?”
“Is there an archer here hight Sam Aylward?” asked a gaunt man-at-arms, clanking up to them across the courtyard.
“My name, friend,” quoth the bow{{ man(Samkin) }}.
“Then sure I have no need to tell thee mine,” said the other.
“By the rood! if it is not Black {{Simon}} of Norwich!” cried Aylward. “A mon coeur, camarade, a mon coeur! Ah, but I am blithe to see thee!” The two fell upon each other and hugged like bears.
“And where from, old blood and bones?” asked the bow{{ man(Samkin) }}.
“I am in service here. Tell me, comrade, is it sooth that we shall have another fling at these French? It is so rumored in the guard-room, and that {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} will take the field once more.”
“It is like enough, {{ mon(Simon) }} gar, as things go.”
“Now may the Lord be praised!” cried the other. “This very night will I set apart a golden ouche to be offered on the shrine of my name-saint. I have pined for this, Aylward, as a young {{ maid(Simon) }} pines for {{ herd(Simon) }} lover.”
“Art so set on plunder then? Is the purse so light that there is not enough for a rouse? I have a bag at my belt, camarade, and you have but to put your fist into it for what you want. It was ever share and share between us.”
“Nay, friend, it is not the French’s gold, but the French’s blood that I would have. I should not rest quiet in the grave, coz, if I had not another turn at them. For with us in France it has ever been fair and honest war—a shut fist for the man, but a bended knee for the woman. But how was it at Winchelsea when their galleys came down upon it some few years back? I had an old mother there, lad, who had come down thither from the Midlands to be the nearer her son. They found her afterwards by her own hearthstone, thrust through by a Frenchman’s bill. My second sister, my brother’s wife, and her two children, they were but ash-heaps in the smoking ruins of their house. I will not say that we have not wrought great scath upon France, but parents and children have been safe from us. And so, old friend, my heart is hot within me, and I long to hear the old battle-cry again, and, by God’s truth! if {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} unfurls {{ hisd(Nigel) }} pennon, here is one who will be right glad to feel the saddle-flaps under {{ hisd(Simon) }} knees.”
“We have seen good work together, old war-dog,” quoth Aylward; “and, by my hilt! we may hope to see more ere we die. But we are more like to hawk at the Spanish woodcock than at the French heron, though certes it is rumored that Du Guesclin with all the best lances of France have taken service under the lions and towers of Castile. But, comrade, it is in my mind that there is some small matter of dispute still open between us.”
“‘Fore God, it is sooth!” cried the other; “I had forgot it. The provost-marshal and her soldiers tore us apart when last we met.”
“On which, friend, we vowed that we should settle the point when next we came together. Hast thy sword, I see, and the moon throws glimmer enough for such old night-birds as we. On guard, {{ mon(Simon) }} gar.! I have not heard clink of steel this month or more.”
“Out from the shadow then,” said the other, drawing {{ hisd(Simon) }} sword. “A vow is a vow, and not lightly to be broken.”
“A vow to the saints,” cried {{Alleyne}}, “is indeed not to be set aside; but this is a devil’s vow, and, simple clerk as I am, I am yet the mouthpiece of the true church when I say that it were mortal sin to fight on such a quarrel. What! shall two grown people carry malice for years, and fly like snarling curs at each other’s throats?”
“No malice, my young clerk, no malice,” quoth Black {{Simon}}. “I have not a bitter drop in my heart for mine old comrade; but the quarrel, as {{ he(Samkind) }} hath told you, is still open and unsettled. Fall on, Aylward!”
“Not whilst I can stand between you,” cried {{Alleyne}}, springing before the bow{{ man(Samkin) }}. “It is shame and sin to see two English Christians turn swords against each other like the frenzied bloodthirsty paynim.”
“And, what is more,” said Hordle {{John}}, suddenly appearing out of the buttery with the huge board upon which the pastry was rolled, “if either raise sword I shall flatten {{ him(Simon) }} like a Shrovetide pancake. By the black rood! I shall drive {{ him(Simon) }} into the earth, like a nail into a door, rather than see you do scath to each other.”
“‘Fore God, this is a strange way of preaching peace,” cried Black {{Simon}}. “You may find the scath yourself, my lusty friend, if you raise your great cudgel to me. I had as lief have the castle drawbridge drop upon my pate.”
“Tell me, Aylward,” said {{Alleyne}} earnestly, with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} hands outstretched to keep the pair asunder, “what is the cause of quarrel, that we may see whether honorable settlement may not be arrived at?”
The bow{{ man(Samkin) }} looked down at {{ hisd(Samkin) }} feet and then up at the moon, “Parbleu!” {{ he(Samkin) }} cried, “the cause of quarrel? Why, {{ mon(Alleyne) }} petit, it was years ago in Limousin, and how can I bear in mind what was the cause of it? {{Simon}} there hath it at the end of {{ hisd(Simon) }} tongue.”
“Not I, in troth,” replied the other; “I have had other things to think of. There was some sort of bickering over dice, or wine, or was it a woman, coz?”
“Pasques Dieu! but you have nicked it,” cried Aylward. “It was indeed about a woman}; and the quarrel must go forward, for I am still of the same mind as before.”
“What of the woman, then?” asked {{Simon}}. “May the murrain strike me if I can call to mind aught about her.”
“It was La Blanche Rose, maid at the sign of the ‘Trois Corbeaux’ at Limoges. Bless her pretty heart! Why, {{ mon(Simon) }} gar., I loved her.”
“So did a many,” quoth {{Simon}}. “I call her to mind now. On the very day that we fought over the little hussy, she went off with Evan ap Price, a long-legged Welsh dagsman. They have a hostel of their own now, somewhere on the banks of the Garonne, where the landlord drinks so much of the liquor that there is little left for the customers.”
“So ends our quarrel, then,” said Aylward, sheathing {{ hisd(Samkinon) }} sword. “A Welsh dagsman, i’ faith! C’etait mauvais gout, camarade, and the more so when she had a jolly archer and a lusty {{ man(Simon) }}-at-arms to choose from.”
“True, old {{ lad(Samkin}}. And it is as well that we can compose our differences honorably, for {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} had been out at the first clash of steel; and {{ he(Nigel) }} hath sworn that if there be quarrelling in the garrison {{ he(Nigel) }} would smite the right hand from the broilers. You know {{ him(Nigel) }} of old, and that {{ he(Nigel) }} is like to be as good as {{ hisd(Nigel) }} word.”
“Mort-Dieu! yes. But there are ale, mead, and wine in the buttery, and the steward a merry rogue, who will not haggle over a quart or two. Buvons, {{ mon(Simonrson) }} gar., for it is not every day that two old friends come together.”
The old soldiers and Hordle {{John}} strode off together in all good fellowship. {{Alleyne}} had turned to follow them, when {{ he(Alleyne) }} felt a touch upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} shoulder, and found a young page by {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} side.
“The {{ Lord(Nigel) }} Loring commands,” said the {{ boy(page) }}, “that you will follow me to the great chamber, and await {{ him(Nigel) }} there.”
“But my comrades?”
“{{ hisd(Nigel) }} commands were for you alone.”
{{Alleyne}} followed the messenger to the east end of the courtyard, where a broad flight of steps led up to the doorway of the main hall, the outer wall of which is washed by the waters of the Avon. As designed at first, no dwelling had been allotted to the owner of the castle and the family but the dark and dismal basement story of the keep. A more civilized or more effeminate generation, however, had refused to be pent up in such a cellar, and the hall with its neighboring chambers had been added for their accommodation. Up the broad steps {{Alleyne}} went, still following {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ boy(page) }}ish guide, until at the folding oak doors the latter paused, and ushered {{ him(Alleyne) }} into the main hall of the castle.
On entering the room the clerk looked round; but, seeing no one, {{ he(Alleyne) }} continued to stand, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} cap in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} hand, examining with the greatest interest a chamber which was so different to any to which {{ he(Alleyne) }} was accustomed. The days had gone by when a noble’s hall was but a barn-like, rush-strewn enclosure, the common lounge and eating-room of every inmate of the castle. The Crusaders had brought back with them experiences of domestic luxuries, of Damascus carpets and rugs of Aleppo, which made them impatient of the hideous bareness and want of privacy which they found in their ancestral strongholds. Still stronger, however, had been the influence of the great French war; for, however well matched the nations might be in martial exercises, there could be no question but that our neighbors were infinitely superior to us in the arts of peace. A stream of returning knights, of wounded soldiers, and of unransomed French nobles, had been for a quarter of a century continually pouring into England, every one of whom exerted an influence in the direction of greater domestic refinement, while shiploads of French furniture from Calais, Rouen, and other plundered towns, had supplied our own artisans with models on which to shape their work. Hence, in most English castles, and in Castle Twynham among the rest, chambers were to be found which would seem to be not wanting either in beauty or in comfort.
In the great stone fireplace a log fire was spurting and crackling, throwing out a ruddy glare which, with the four bracket-lamps which stood at each corner of the room, gave a bright and lightsome air to the whole apartment. Above was a wreath-work of blazonry, extending up to the carved and corniced oaken roof; while on either side stood the high canopied chairs placed for the {{ master(Nigel) }} of the house and for {{ hisd(Nigel) }} most honored guest. The walls were hung all round with most elaborate and brightly colored tapestry, representing the achievements of Dame Beverly of Hampton, and behind this convenient screen were stored the tables dormant and benches which would be needed for banquet or high festivity. The floor was of polished tiles, with a square of red and black diapered Flemish carpet in the centre; and many settees, cushions, folding chairs, and carved bancals littered all over it. At the further end was a long black buffet or dresser, thickly covered with gold cups, silver salvers, and other such valuables.
All this {{Alleyne}} examined with curious eyes; but most interesting of all to {{ him(Alleyne) }} was a small ebony table at {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} very side, on which, by the side of a chess-board and the scattered chessmen, there lay an open manuscript written in a right clerkly hand, and set forth with brave flourishes and devices along the margins. In vain {{Alleyne}} bethought {{ him(Alleyne) }} of where {{ he(Alleyne) }} was, and of those laws of good breeding and decorum which should restrain {{ him(Alleyne) }}: those colored capitals and black even lines drew {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} hand down to them, as the loadstone draws the needle, until, almost before {{ he(Alleyne) }} knew it, {{ he(Alleyne) }} was standing with the romance of Garin de Montglane before {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} eyes, so absorbed in its contents as to be completely oblivious both of where {{ he(Alleyne) }} was and why {{ he(Alleyne) }} had come there.
{{ He(Alleyne) }} was brought back to {{ himself(Alleyne) }}, however, by a sudden little ripple of quick {{ feminine(Maude) }} laughter. Aghast, {{ he(Alleyne) }} dropped the manuscript among the chessmen and stared in bewilderment round the room. It was as empty and as still as ever. Beautiful as {{ she(Maude) }} had seemed to {{ him(Alleyne) }} before, the lithe charm of {{ herd(Maude) }} figure and the proud, free grace of {{ herd(Maude) }} bearing were enhanced now by the rich simplicity of {{ herd(Maude) }} attire.

Oh that I were a minstrel, that I might put it into rhyme, with the whole romance—the luckless {{ maid(Maude) }}, the wicked socman, and the virtuous clerk! “What I did,” said {{Alleyne}}, “was too small a thing for thanks; and yet, if I may say it without offence, it was too grave and near a matter for mirth and raillery. “God help me! I am the weakest of the weak,” groaned {{Alleyne}}. “I pray that I may have more strength.”
“And to what end?” {{ she(Maude) }} asked sharply. “If you are, as I understand, to shut yourself forever in your cell within the four walls of an abbey, then of what use would it be were your prayer to be answered?”
“The use of my own salvation.”
{{ She(Maude) }} turned from {{ him(Alleyne) }} with a pretty shrug and wave. “Is that all?” {{ she(Maude) }} said. “Then you are no better than {{ Father(Christopher) }} {{Christopher}} and the rest of them. My {{ father(Nigel) }} is the {{ king(Edward}}’s {{ man(Nigel) }}, and when {{ he(Nigel) }} rides into the press of fight {{ he(Nigel) }} is not thinking ever of the saving of {{ hisd(Nigel) }} own poor body; {{ he(Nigel) }} recks little enough if {{ he(Nigel) }} leave it on the field. “There is sooth in what you say, {{ lady(Maude) }},” {{Alleyne}} answered; “and yet I scarce can see what you would have the clergy and the church to do.”
“I would have them live as others and do honest work in the world, preaching by their lives rather than their words. “On citole, flute and rebeck.”

But there is no call for haste. Rest upon it for the night, and let your orisons ask for guidance in the matter. “Little enough, {{ lady(Mary) }}, compared with those who were my teachers.”
“Yet enough for my purpose, I doubt not. And so {{Alleyne}} found {{ himself(Alleyne) }} not only chosen as squire to a knight but also as squire to three damosels, which was even further from the part which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had thought to play in the world. <h2>Chapter XII. How {{Alleyne}} Learned More Than He Could Teach</h2>
And now there came a time of stir and bustle, of furbishing of arms and clang of hammer from all the southland counties. Fast spread the tidings from thorpe to thorpe and from castle to castle, that the old game was afoot once more, and the lions and lilies to be in the field with the early spring. Great news this for that fierce old country, whose trade for a generation had been war, her exports archers and her imports prisoners. For six years her children had chafed under an unwonted peace. Now they flew to their arms as to their birthright. The old soldiers of Crecy, of Nogent, and of Poictiers were glad to think that they might hear the war-trumpet once more, and gladder still were the hot youth who had chafed for years under the martial tales of their sires. To pierce the great mountains of the south, to fight the tamers of the fiery Moors, to follow the greatest captain of the age, to find sunny cornfields and vineyards, when the marches of Picardy and Normandy were as rare and bleak as the Jedburgh forests—here was a golden prospect for a race of warriors. From sea to sea there was stringing of bows in the cottage and clang of steel in the castle.
Nor did it take long for every stronghold to pour forth its cavalry, and every hamlet its footmen. Through the late autumn and the early winter every road and country lane resounded with nakir and trumpet, with the neigh of the war-horse and the clatter of marching boots. From the Wrekin in the Welsh marches to the Cotswolds in the west or Butser in the south, there was no hill-top from which the peasant might not have seen the bright shimmer of arms, the toss and flutter of plume and of pensil. From bye-path, from woodland clearing, or from winding moor-side track these little rivulets of steel united in the larger roads to form a broader stream, growing ever fuller and larger as it approached the nearest or most commodious seaport. And there all day, and day after day, there was bustle and crowding and labor, while the great ships loaded up, and one after the other spread their white pinions and darted off to the open sea, amid the clash of cymbals and rolling of drums and lusty shouts of those who went and of those who waited. From Orwell to the Dart there was no port which did not send forth its little fleet, gay with streamer and bunting, as for a joyous festival. Thus in the season of the waning days the might of England put forth on to the waters.
In the ancient and populous county of Hampshire there was no lack of leaders or of soldiers for a service which promised either honor or profit. In the north the Saracen’s head of the Brocas and the scarlet fish of the De Roches were waving over a strong body of archers from Holt, Woolmer, and Harewood forests. De Borhunte was up in the east, and Dame Anne de Montague in the west. Sir Luke de Ponynges, Sir Thomas West, Dame Agnes de Bruin, {{ Sirr(Arthur) }} {{Arthur}} Lipscombe, Dame Sarah Ramsey, and stout {{ Sirr(Oliver) }} {{Oliver}} Buttesthorn were all marching south with levies from Andover, Arlesford, Odiham and Winchester, while from Sussex came Dame Alice Clinton, Dame Martha Cheyne, and Sir John Fallislee, with a troop of picked men-at-arms, making for their port at Southampton. Greatest of all the musters, however, was that of Twynham Castle, for the name and the fame of {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} Loring drew towards {{ him(Nigel) }} the keenest and boldest spirits, all eager to serve under so valiant a leader. Archers from the New Forest and the Forest of Bere, billmen from the pleasant country which is watered by the Stour, the Avon, and the Itchen, young cavaliers from the ancient Hampshire houses, all were pushing for Christchurch to take service under the banner of the five scarlet roses.
And now, could {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} have shown the bachelles of land which the laws of rank required, {{ he(Nigel) }} might well have cut {{ hisd(Nigel) }} forked pennon into a square banner, and taken such a following into the field as would have supported the dignity of a banneret. But poverty was heavy upon {{ him(Nigel) }}, {{ hisd(Nigel) }} land was scant, {{ hisd(Nigel) }} coffers empty, and the very castle which covered {{ him(Nigel) }} the holding of another. Sore was {{ hisd(Nigel) }} heart when {{ he(Nigel) }} saw rare bowmen and war-hardened spearmen turned away from {{ hisd(Nigel) }} gates, for the lack of the money which might equip and pay them. Yet the letter which Aylward had brought {{ him(Nigel) }} gave {{ him(Nigel) }} powers which {{ he(Nigel) }} was not slow to use. In it {{ Sirr(Claude) }} {{Claude}} Latour, the Gascon lieutenant of the White Company, assured {{ him(Nigel) }} that there remained in {{ hisd(Claude) }} keeping enough to fit out a hundred archers and twenty men-at-arms, which, joined to the three hundred veteran companions already in France, would make a force which any leader might be proud to command. Carefully and sagaciously the veteran knight chose out soldiers from the swarm of volunteers. Many an anxious consultation {{ he(Nigel) }} held with Black {{Simon}}, Sam Aylward, and other of {{ hisd(Nigel) }} more experienced followers, as to who should come and who should stay. By All Saints’ day, however ere the last leaves had fluttered to earth in the Wilverley and Holmesley glades, {{ he(Nigel) }} had filled up {{ hisd(Nigel) }} full numbers, and mustered under {{ hisd(Nigel) }} banner as stout a following of Hampshire foresters as ever twanged their war-bows. Twenty men-at-arms, too, well mounted and equipped, formed the cavalry of the party, while young {{Peter}} Terlake of Fareham, and {{Walter}} Ford of Botley, the martial sons of martial sires, came at their own cost to wait upon {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} and to share with {{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }} the duties of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} squireship.
Yet, even after the enrolment, there was much to be done ere the party could proceed upon its way. For armor, swords, and lances, there was no need to take much forethought, for they were to be had both better and cheaper in Bordeaux than in England. With the long-bow, however, it was different. Yew staves indeed might be got in Spain, but it was well to take enough and to spare with them. Then three spare cords should be carried for each bow, with a great store of arrow-heads, besides the brigandines of chain mail, the wadded steel caps, and the brassarts or arm-guards, which were the proper equipment of the archer. Above all, the peasents for miles round were hard at work cutting the white surcoats which were the badge of the Company, and adorning them with the red lion of St. George upon the centre of the breast. When all was completed and the muster called in the castle yard the oldest soldier of the French wars was fain to confess that {{ he(Nigel) }} had never looked upon a better equipped or more warlike body of fighters, from the old knight with {{ hisd(Nigel) }} silk jupon, sitting {{ hisd(Nigel) }} great black war-horse in the front of them, to Hordle {{John}}, the giant recruit, who leaned carelessly upon a huge black bow-stave in the rear. Of the six score, fully half had seen service before, while a fair sprinkling were those who had followed the wars all their lives, and had a hand in those battles which had made the whole world ring with the fame and the wonder of the island infantry.
Six long weeks were taken in these preparations, and it was close on Martinmas ere all was ready for a start. Nigh two months had {{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }} been in Castle Twynham—months which were fated to turn the whole current of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} life, to divert it from that dark and lonely bourne towards which it tended, and to guide it into freer and more sunlit channels. Already {{ he(Alleyne) }} had learned to bless {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ father(Edric) }} for that wise provision which had made {{ him(Alleyne) }} seek to know the world ere {{ he(Alleyne) }} had ventured to renounce it.
For it was a different place from that which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had pictured—very different from that which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had heard described when the {{ master(Jerome) }}  of the novices held forth to {{ hisd(Jerome) }} charges upon the ravening wolves who lurked for them beyond the peaceful folds of Beaulieu. There was cruelty in it, doubtless, and lust and sin and sorrow; but were there not virtues to atone, robust positive virtues which did not shrink from temptation, which held their own in all the rough blasts of the work-a-day world? How colorless by contrast appeared the sinlessness which came from inability to sin, the conquest which was attained by flying from the enemy! {{ Monk(Berghersh) }}-bred as {{ he(Alleyne) }} was, {{Alleyne}} had native shrewdness and a mind which was young enough to form new conclusions and to outgrow old ones. {{ He(Alleyne) }} could not fail to see that those with whom {{ he(Alleyne) }} was thrown in contact, rough-tongued, fierce and quarrelsome as they were, were yet of deeper nature and of more service in the world than the ox-eyed brethren who rose and ate and slept from year’s end to year’s end in their own narrow, stagnant circle of existence. {{ Abbot(Berghersh) }} Berghersh was a good {{ man(Berghersh) }}, but how was {{ he(Berghersh) }} better than this kindly knight, who lived as simple a life, held as lofty and inflexible an ideal of duty, and did with all {{ hisd(Nigel) }} fearless heart whatever came to {{ hisd(Nigel) }} hand to do? In turning from the service of the one to that of the other, {{Alleyne}} could not feel that {{ he(Alleyne) }} was lowering {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} aims in life. True that {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} gentle and thoughtful nature recoiled from the grim work of war, yet in those days of martial orders and militant brotherhoods there was no gulf fixed betwixt the priest and the soldier. The {{ man(Alleyne) }} of God and the {{ man(Alleyne) }} of the sword might without scandal be united in the same individual. Why then should {{ he(Alleyne) }}, a mere clerk, have scruples when so fair a chance lay in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} way of carrying out the spirit as well as the letter of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ father(Edric) }}’s provision. Henceforth for seven hours a day {{ he(Alleyne) }} strove in the tilt-yard to qualify {{ himself(Alleyne) }} to be a worthy squire to so worthy a knight. Young, supple and active, with all the pent energies from years of pure and healthy living, it was not long before {{ he(Alleyne) }} could manage {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} horse and {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} weapon well enough to earn an approving nod from critical men-at-arms, or to hold {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} own against Terlake and Ford, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} fellow-servitors.
But were there no other considerations which swayed {{ him(Alleyne) }} from the cloisters towards the world? So complex is the human spirit that it can itself scarce discern the deep springs which impel it to action. Yet to {{Alleyne}} had been opened now a side of life of which {{ he(Alleyne) }} had been as innocent as a child, but one which was of such deep import that it could not fail to influence {{ him(Alleyne) }} in choosing {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} path. Fair {{ maid(Maude) }}s in monkish precepts, had been the embodiment and concentration of what was dangerous and evil—a focus whence spread all that was to be dreaded and avoided. <h2>Chapter XIII How the White Company Set Forth to the Wars</h2>
St. Luke’s day had come and had gone, and it was in the season of Martinmas, when the oxen are driven in to the slaughter, that the White Company was ready for its journey. Loud shrieked the brazen bugles from keep and from gateway, and merry was the rattle of the war-drum, as the men and women gathered in the outer bailey, with torches to light them, for the morn had not yet broken. {{Alleyne}}, from the window of the armory, looked down upon the strange scene—the circles of yellow flickering light, the lines of stern and bearded faces, the quick shimmer of arms, and the lean heads of the horses. In front stood the bow-men, ten deep, with a fringe of under-officers, who paced hither and thither marshalling the ranks with curt precept or short rebuke. Behind were the little clump of steel-clad horsemen, their lances raised, with long pensils drooping down the oaken shafts. So silent and still were they, that they might have been metal-sheathed statues, were it not for the occasional quick, impatient stamp of their chargers, or the rattle of chamfron against neck-plates as they tossed and strained. A spear’s length in front of them sat the spare and long-limbed figure of Black Simon, the Norwich fighting {{ man(Simon) }}, {{ hisd(Simon) }} fierce, deep-lined face framed in steel, and the silk guidon marked with the five scarlet roses slanting over {{ hisd(Simon) }} right shoulder. All round, in the edge of the circle of the light, stood the castle servants, the soldiers who were to form the garrison, and little knots of family, who sobbed and called shrilly to their name-saints to watch over the Wat, or {{Will}}, or Peterkin who had turned their hand to the work of war.
The young squire was leaning forward, gazing at the stirring and martial scene, when {{ he(Alleyne) }} heard a short, quick gasp at {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} shoulder, and there was the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}}, with {{ herd(Maude) }} hand to {{ herd(Maude) }} heart, leaning up against the wall, slender and fair, like a half-plucked lily. {{ Herd(Maude) }} face was turned away from {{ him(Alleyne) }}, but {{ he(Alleyne) }} could see, by the sharp intake of {{ herd(Maude) }} breath, that {{ she(Maude) }} was weeping bitterly.
“Alas! alas!” {{ he(Alleyne) }} cried, all unnerved at the sight, “why is it that you are so sad, {{ lady(Maude) }}?”
“It is the sight of these brave soldiers,” {{ she(Maude) }} answered; “and to think how many of them go and how few are like to find their way back. I have seen it before, when I was a little {{ maid(Maude) }}, in the year of the {{ Prince(Edwards) }}’s great battle. I remember then how they mustered in the bailey, even as they do now, and my {{ lady(Mary) }}-{{ mother(Mary) }} holding me in {{ herd(Mary) }} arms at this very window that I might see the show.”
“Please God, you will see them all back ere another year be out,” said {{ he(Alleyne) }}.
{{ She(Maude) }} shook {{ herd(Maude) }} head, looking round at {{ him(Alleyne) }} with flushed cheeks and eyes that sparkled in the lamp-light. “Oh, but I hate myself for being a gentle{{ woman(Maude) }}!” {{ she(Maude) }} cried, with a stamp of {{ herd(Maude) }} little foot. “What can I do that is good? Here I must bide, and talk and read, and read and talk. Ever the same dull round, with nothing at the end of it. And now you are going too, who could carry my thoughts out of these gray walls, and raise my mind above tapestry and distaffs. What can I do? I am of no more use or value than that broken bowstave.”
“You are of such value to me,” {{ he(Alleyne) }} cried, in a whirl of hot, passionate words, “that all else has become nought. You are my heart, my life, my one and only thought. Oh, {{Maude}}, I cannot live without you, I cannot leave you without a word of love. All is changed to me since I have known you. I am poor and lowly and all unworthy of you; but if great love may weigh down such defects, then mine may do it. Give me but one word of hope to take to the wars with me—but one. Ah, you shrink, you shudder! My wild words have frightened you.”
Twice {{ she(Maude) }} opened {{ herd(Maude) }} lips, and twice no sound came from them. At last {{ she(Maude) }} spoke in a hard and measured voice, as one who dare not trust {{ herself(Maude) }} to speak too freely.
“This is over sudden,” {{ she(Maude) }} said; “it is not so long since the world was nothing to you. You have changed once; perchance you may change again.”
“Cruel!” {{ he(Alleyne) }} cried, “who hath changed me?”
“And then your {{ brother(Simon) }},” {{ she(Maude) }} continued with a little laugh, disregarding {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} question. “Methinks this hath become a family custom amongst the {{Edric}}kin. Nay, I am sorry; I did not mean a jibe. But, indeed, {{Alleyne}}, this hath come suddenly upon me, and I scarce know what to say.”
“Say some word of hope, however distant—some kind word that I may cherish in my heart.”
“Nay, {{Alleyne}}, it were a cruel kindness, and you have been too good and true a friend to me that I should use you despitefully. There cannot be a closer link between us. It is madness to think of it. Were there no other reasons, it is enough that my {{ father(Nigel) }} and your {{ brother(Simon) }} would both cry out against it.”
“My {{ brother(Simon) }}, what has {{ he(Simon) }} to do with it? And your {{ father(Nigel) }}——”
“Come, {{Alleyne}}, was it not you who would have me act fairly to all, and, certes, to my {{ father(Nigel) }} amongst them?”
“You say truly,” {{ he(Alleyne) }} cried, “you say truly. But you do not reject me, {{Maude}}? You give me some ray of hope? I do not ask pledge or promise. Say only that I am not hateful to you—that on some happier day I may hear kinder words from you.”
{{ Herd(Maude) }} eyes softened upon {{ him(Alleyne) }}, and a kind answer was on {{ herd(Maude) }} lips, when a hoarse shout, with the clatter of arms and stamping of steeds, rose up from the bailey below. At the sound {{ herd(Maude) }} face set {{ herd(Maude) }} eyes sparkled, and {{ she(Maude) }} stood with flushed cheek and head thrown back—a {{ woman(Maude) }}’s body, with a soul of fire.
“My {{ father(Nigel) }} hath gone down,” {{ she(Maude) }} cried. “Your place is by {{ hisd(Nigel) }} side. Nay, look not at me, {{Alleyne}}. It is no time for dallying.
