“'God be with thee, my honored {{ lord(Nigel) }}, and have thee in His holy keeping. The {{ Lady(Mary) }} Loring hath asked me to set down in writing what hath befallen at Twynham, and all that concerns the death of thy ill neighbor the Socman of Minstead. For when ye had left us, this evil {{ man(Simon) }} gathered around {{ him(Simon) }} all outlaws, villeins, and masterless folk, until they were come to such a force that they slew and scattered the {{ king(Edward) }}'s men who went against them. Then, coming forth from the woods, they laid siege to thy castle, and for two days they girt us in and shot hard against us, with such numbers as were a marvel to see. Yet the {{ Lady(Mary) }} Loring held the place stoutly, and on the second day the Socman was slain—by {{ hisd(Simon) }} own followers, as some think—so that we were delivered from their hands; for which praise be to all the saints, and more especially to the holy Anselm, upon whose feast it came to pass. The {{ Lady(Mary) }} Loring, and the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}}, thy fair {{ daughter(Maude) }}, are in good health; and so also am I, save for an imposthume of the toe-joint, which hath been sent me for my sins. May all the saints preserve thee!'”
“It was the vision of the {{ Lady(Mary) }} Tiphain,” said {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, after a pause. “Marked you not how {{ she(Mary) }} said that the leader was one with a yellow {{ beard(Simon) }}, and how {{ he(Simon) }} fell before the gate. But how came it, {{Alleyne}}, that this {{ woman(Mary) }}, to whom all things are as crystal, and who hath not said one word which has not come to pass, was yet so led astray as to say that your thoughts turned to Twynham Castle even more than my own?”
“My fair {{ lord(Nigel) }},” said {{Alleyne}}, with a flush on {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} weather-stained cheeks, “the {{ Lady(Mary) }} Tiphain may have spoken sooth when {{ she(Mary) }} said it; for Twynham Castle is in my heart by day and in my dreams by night.”
“Ha!” cried {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, with a sidelong glance.
“Yes, my fair {{ lord(Nigel) }}; for indeed I love your {{ daughter(Maude) }}, the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}}; and, unworthy as I am, I would give my heart's blood to serve {{ her(Maude) }}.”
“By St. Paul! {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }},” said the knight coldly, arching {{ hisd(Nigel) }} eyebrows, “you aim high in this matter. Our blood is very old.”
“And mine also is very old,” answered the squire.
“And the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}} is our single child. All our name and lands centre upon {{ her(Maude) }}.”
“Alas! that I should say it, but I also am now the only {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }}.”
“And why have I not heard this from you before, {{Alleyne}}? In sooth, I think that you have used me ill.”
“Nay, my fair {{ lord(Nigel) }}, say not so; for I know not whether your {{ daughter(Maude) }} loves me, and there is no pledge between us.”
{{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} pondered for a few moments, and then burst out a-laughing. “By St. Paul!” said {{ he(Nigel) }}, “I know not why I should mix in the matter; for I have ever found that the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}} was very well able to look to {{ herd(Maude) }} own affairs. Since first {{ she(Maude) }} could stamp {{ herd(Maude) }} little foot, {{ she(Maude) }} hath ever been able to get that for which {{ she(Maude) }} craved; and if {{ she(Maude) }} set {{ herd(Maude) }} heart on thee, {{Alleyne}}, and thou on {{ her(Maude) }}, I do not think that this Spanish {{ king(Charles) }}, with {{ hisd(Charles) }} three-score thousand men, could hold you apart. Yet this I will say, that I would see you a full knight ere you go to my {{ daughter(Maude) }} with words of love. I have ever said that a brave lance should wed {{ her(Maude) }}; and, by my soul! {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }}, if God spare you, I think that you will acquit yourself well. But enough of such trifles, for we have our work before us, and it will be time to speak of this matter when we see the white cliffs of England once more. Go to {{ Sirr(William) }} {{William}} Felton, I pray you, and ask {{ him(William) }} to come hither, for it is time that we were marching. There is no pass at the further end of the valley, and it is a perilous place should an enemy come upon us.”
{{Alleyne}} delivered the message, and then wandered forth from the camp, for {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} mind was all in a whirl with this unexpected news, and with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} talk with {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}. Sitting upon a rock, with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} burning brow resting upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} hands, {{ he(Alleyne) }} thought of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ brother(Simon) }}, of their quarrel, of the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}} in {{ herd(Maude) }} bedraggled riding-dress, of the gray old castle, of the proud pale face in the armory, and of the last fiery words with which {{ she(Maude) }} had sped {{ him(Alleyne) }} on {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} way. Then {{ he(Alleyne) }} was but a penniless, church-bred {{ lad(Alleyne) }}, unknown and unfriended. Now {{ he(Alleyne) }} was {{ himself(Alleyne) }} Socman of Minstead, the head of an old stock, and the {{ lord(Alleyne) }} of an estate which, if reduced from its former size, was still ample to preserve the dignity of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} family. Further, {{ he(Alleyne) }} had become a {{ man(Alleyne) }} of experience, was counted brave among the brave, had won the esteem and confidence of {{ herd(Maude) }} {{ father(Nigel) }}, and, above all, had been listened to by {{ him(Nigel) }} when {{ he(Alleyne) }} told {{ him(Nigel) }} the secret of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} love. As to the gaining of knighthood, in such stirring times it was no great matter for a brave squire of gentle birth to aspire to that honor. {{ He(Alleyne) }} would leave {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} bones among these Spanish ravines, or {{ he(Alleyne) }} would do some deed which would call the eyes of all upon {{ him(Alleyne) }}.
{{Alleyne}} was still seated on the rock, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} griefs and {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} joys drifting swiftly over {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} mind like the shadow of clouds upon a sunlit meadow, when of a sudden {{ he(Alleyne) }} became conscious of a low, deep sound which came booming up to {{ him(Alleyne) }} through the fog. Close behind {{ him(Alleyne) }} {{ he(Alleyne) }} could hear the murmur of the bowmen, the occasional bursts of hoarse laughter, and the champing and stamping of their horses. Behind it all, however, came that low-pitched, deep-toned hum, which seemed to come from every quarter and to fill the whole air. In the old monastic days {{ he(Alleyne) }} remembered to have heard such a sound when {{ he(Alleyne) }} had walked out one windy night at Bucklershard, and had listened to the long waves breaking upon the shingly shore. Here, however, was neither wind nor sea, and yet the dull murmur rose ever louder and stronger out of the heart of the rolling sea of vapor. {{ He(Alleyne) }} turned and ran to the camp, shouting an alarm at the top of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} voice.
It was but a hundred paces, and yet ere {{ he(Alleyne) }} had crossed it every archer was ready at their horses' heads, and the group of knights were out and listening intently to the ominous sound.
“It is a great body of horse,” said {{ Sirr(William) }} {{William}} Felton, “and they are riding very swiftly hitherwards.”
“Yet they must be from the {{ prince(Edwards) }}'s army,” remarked {{ Sirr(Richard) }} {{Richard}} Causton, “for they come from the north.”
“Nay,” said the {{ Earl(Thomas) }} of Angus, “it is not so certain; for the peasant with whom we spoke last night said that it was rumored that {{ Ddon(Tello) }} {{Tello}}, the Spanish {{ king(Charles) }}'s {{ sister(Tello) }}, had ridden with six thousand chosen fighters to beat up the {{ prince(Edwards) }}'s camp. It may be that on their backward road they have come this way.”
“By St. Paul!” cried {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, “I think that it is even as you say, for that same peasant had a sour face and a shifting eye, as one who bore us little good will. I doubt not that he has brought these cavaliers upon us.”
“But the mist covers us,” said {{ Sirr(Simon) }} {{Simon}} Burley. “We have yet time to ride through the further end of the pass.”
“Were we a troop of mountain goats we might do so,” answered {{ Sirr(William) }} {{William}} Felton, “but it is not to be passed by a company of horsemen. If these be indeed {{ Ddon(Tello) }} {{Tello}} and {{ hisd(Tello) }} forces, then we must bide where we are, and do what we can to make them rue the day that they found us in their path.”
“Well spoken, {{William}}!” cried {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, in high delight. “If there be so many as has been said, then there will be much honor to be gained from them and every hope of advancement. But the sound has ceased, and I fear that they have gone some other way.”
“Or mayhap they have come to the mouth of the gorge, and are marshalling their ranks. Hush and hearken! for they are no great way from us.”
The Company stood peering into the dense fog-wreath, amidst a silence so profound that the dripping of the water from the rocks and the breathing of the horses grew loud upon the ear. Suddenly from out the sea of mist came the shrill sound of a neigh, followed by a long blast upon a bugle.
“It is a Spanish call, my fair {{ lord(Nigel) }},” said Black {{Simon}}. “It is used by their prickers and huntsmen when the beast hath not fled, but is still in its lair.”
“By my faith!” said {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, smiling, “if they are in a humor for venerie we may promise them some sport ere they sound the mort over us. But there is a hill in the centre of the gorge on which we might take our stand.”
“I marked it yester-night,” said Felton, “and no better spot could be found for our purpose, for it is very steep at the back. It is but a bow-shot to the left, and, indeed, I can see the shadow of it.”
The whole Company, leading their horses, passed across to the small hill which loomed in front of them out of the mist. It was indeed admirably designed for defence, for it sloped down in front, all jagged and boulder-strewn, while it fell away in a sheer cliff of a hundred feet or more. On the summit was a small uneven plateau, with a stretch across of a hundred paces, and a depth of half as much again.
“Unloose the horses!” said {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}. “We have no space for them, and if we hold our own we shall have horses and to spare when this day's work is done. Nay, keep yours, my fair sirs, for we may have work for them. Aylward, Johnston, let your archers form a harrow on either side of the ridge. {{ Sirr(Oliver) }} {{Oliver}} and you, my {{ Lord(Thomas) }} Angus, I give you the right wing, and the left to you, {{ Sirr(Simon) }} {{Simon}}, and to you, {{ Sirr(Richard) }} {{ Richard}} Causton. I and {{ Sirr(William) }} {{William}} Felton will hold the centre with our forces. Now order the ranks, and fling wide the banners, for our souls are God's and our bodies the {{ king(Edward) }}'s, and our swords for Saint George and for England!”
{{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} had scarcely spoken when the mist seemed to thin in the valley, and to shred away into long ragged clouds which trailed from the edges of the cliffs. The gorge in which they had camped was a mere wedge-shaped cleft among the hills, three-quarters of a mile deep, with the small rugged rising upon which they stood at the further end, and the brown crags walling it in on three sides. As the mist parted, and the sun broke through, it gleamed and shimmered with dazzling brightness upon the armor and headpieces of a vast body of horsemen who stretched across the barranca from one cliff to the other, and extended backwards until their rear guard were far out upon the plain beyond. Line after line, and rank after rank, they choked the neck of the valley with a long vista of tossing pennons, twinkling lances, waving plumes and streaming banderoles, while the curvets and gambades of the chargers lent a constant motion and shimmer to the glittering, many-colored mass. A yell of exultation, and a forest of waving steel through the length and breadth of their column, announced that they could at last see their entrapped enemies, while the swelling notes of a hundred bugles and drums, mixed with the clash of Moorish cymbals, broke forth into a proud peal of martial triumph. Strange it was to these gallant and sparkling cavaliers of Spain to look upon this handful of fighters upon the hill, the thin lines of bowmen, the knots of knights and men-at-arms with armor rusted and discolored from long service, and to learn that these were indeed the soldiers whose fame and prowess had been the camp-fire talk of every army in Christendom. Very still and silent they stood, leaning upon their bows, while their leaders took counsel together in front of them. No clang of bugle rose from their stern ranks, but in the centre waved the leopards of England, on the right the ensign of their Company with the roses of Loring, and on the left, over three score of Welsh bowmen, there floated the red banner of Merlin with the boars'-heads of the Buttesthorns. Gravely and sedately they stood beneath the morning sun waiting for the onslaught of their foemen.
“By Saint Paul!” said {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, gazing with puckered eye down the valley, “there appear to be some very worthy people among them. What is this golden banner which waves upon the left?”
“It is the ensign of the Knights of Calatrava,” answered Felton.
“And the other upon the right?”
“It marks the Knights of Santiago, and I see by the flag that their grand-master rides at their head. There too is the banner of Castile amid yonder sparkling squadron which heads the main battle. There are six thousand soldiers with ten squadrons of slingers as far as I may judge their numbers.”
“There are Frenchmen among them, my fair {{ lord(William) }},” remarked Black {{Simon}}. “I can see the pennons of De Couvette, De Brieux, Saint Pol, and many others who struck in against us for Charles of Blois.”
“You are right,” said {{ Sirr(William) }} {{William}}, “for I can also see them. There is much Spanish blazonry also, if I could but read it. {{ Ddon(Diego) }} {{Diego}}, you know the arms of your own land. Who are they who have done us this honor?”
The Spanish prisoner looked with exultant eyes upon the deep and serried ranks of {{ hisd(Diego) }} countrymen.
“By Saint James!” said {{ he(Diego) }}, “if ye fall this day ye fall by no mean hands, for the flower of the knighthood of Castile ride under the banner of {{ Ddon(Tello) }} Tello, with the chivalry of Asturias, Toledo, Leon, Cordova, Galicia, and Seville. I see the guidons of Albornez, Cacorla, Rodriguez, Tavora, with the two great orders, and the knights of France and of Aragon. If you will take my rede you will come to a composition with them, for they will give you such terms as you have given me.”
“Nay, by Saint Paul! it were pity if so many brave souls were drawn together, and no little deed of arms to come of it. Ha! {{William}}, they advance upon us; and, by my soul! it is a sight that is worth coming over the seas to see.”
As {{ he(Simon) }} spoke, the two wings of the Spanish host, consisting of the Knights of Calatrava on the one side and of Santiago upon the other, came swooping swiftly down the valley, while the main body followed more slowly behind. Five hundred paces from the English the two great bodies of horse crossed each other, and, sweeping round in a curve, retired in feigned confusion towards their centre. Often in bygone wars had the Moors tempted the hot-blooded Spaniards from their places of strength by such pretended flights, but there were people upon the hill to whom every ruse and trick of war were as their daily trade and practice. Again and even nearer came the rallying Spaniards, and again with cry of fear and stooping bodies they swerved off to right and left, but the English still stood stolid and observant among their rocks. The vanguard halted a long bow shot from the hill, and with waving spears and vaunting shouts challenged their enemies to come forth, while two cavaliers, pricking forward from the glittering ranks, walked their horses slowly between the two arrays with targets braced and lances in rest like the challengers in a tourney.
“By Saint Paul!” cried {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, with {{ hisd(Nigel) }} one eye glowing like an ember, “these appear to be two very worthy and debonair fighters. I do not call to mind when I have seen any people who seemed of so great a heart and so high of enterprise. We have our horses, {{ Sirr(William) }} {{William}}: shall we not relieve them of any vow which they may have upon their souls?”
Felton's reply was to bound upon {{ hisd(William) }} charger, and to urge it down the slope, while {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} followed not three spears'-lengths behind {{ him(William) }}. It was a rugged course, rocky and uneven, yet the two knights, choosing their retainers, dashed onwards at the top of their speed, while the gallant Spaniards flew as swiftly to meet them. The one to whom Felton found {{ himself(William) }} opposed was a tall stripling with a stag's head upon his shield, while {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}'s foe was a broad and squat {{ man(Sebastian) }} with a plain steel harness, and a pink and white torse bound round {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} helmet. The first struck Felton on the target with such force as to split it from side to side, but {{ Sirr(William) }} {{William}}'s lance crashed through the camail which shielded the Spaniard's throat, and he} fell, screaming hoarsely, to the ground. Carried away by the heat and madness of fight, the English knight never drew rein, but charged straight on into the array of the knights of Calatrava. Long time the silent ranks upon the hill could see a swirl and eddy deep down in the heart of the Spanish column, with a circle of rearing chargers and flashing blades. Here and there tossed the white plume of the English helmet, rising and falling like the foam upon a wave, with the fierce gleam and sparkle ever circling round it until at last it had sunk from view, and another brave soul had turned from war to peace.
{{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, meanwhile, had found a foe worthy of {{ hisd(Nigel) }} steel for {{ hisd(Nigel) }} opponent was none other than {{Sebastian}} Gomez, the picked lance of the monkish Knights of Santiago, who had won fame in a hundred bloody combats with the Moors of Andalusia. So fierce was their meeting that their spears shivered up to the very grasp, and the horses reared backwards until it seemed that they must crash down upon their riders. Yet with consummate horsemanship they both swung round in a long curvet, and then plucking out their swords they lashed at each other like two lusty smiths hammering upon an anvil. The chargers spun round each other, biting and striking, while the two blades wheeled and whizzed and circled in gleams of dazzling light. Cut, parry, and thrust followed so swiftly upon each other that the eye could not follow them, until at last coming thigh to thigh, they cast their arms around each other and rolled off their saddles to the ground. The heavier Spaniard threw {{ himself(Sebastian) }} upon {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} enemy, and pinning {{ him(Nigel) }} down beneath {{ him(Sebastian) }} raised {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} sword to slay {{ him(Nigel) }}, while a shout of triumph rose from the ranks of {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} countrymen. But the fatal blow never fell, for even as {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} arm quivered before descending, the Spaniard gave a shudder, and stiffening {{ himself(Sebastian) }} rolled heavily over upon {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} side, with the blood gushing from {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} armpit and from the slit of {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} vizor. {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} sprang to {{ hisd(Nigel) }} feet with {{ hisd(Nigel) }} bloody dagger in {{ hisd(Nigel) }} left hand and gazed down upon {{ hisd(Nigel) }} adversary, but that fatal and sudden stab in the vital spot, which the Spaniard had exposed by raising {{ hisd(Sebastian) }} arm, had proved instantly mortal. The English{{ man(Nigel) }} leaped upon {{ hisd(Nigel) }} horse and made for the hill, at the very instant that a yell of rage from a thousand voices and the clang of a score of bugles announced the Spanish onset.
But the islanders were ready and eager for the encounter. With feet firmly planted, their sleeves rolled back to give free play to their muscles, their long yellow bow-staves in their left hands, and their quivers slung to the front, they had waited in the four-deep harrow formation which gave strength to their array, and yet permitted each to draw an arrow freely without harm to those in front. Aylward and Johnston had been engaged in throwing light tufts of grass into the air to gauge the wind force, and a hoarse whisper passed down the ranks from the file-leaders to the ranks, with scraps of advice and admonition.
“Do not shoot outside the fifteen-score paces,” cried Johnston. “We may need all our shafts ere we have done with them.”
“Better to overshoot than to undershoot,” added Aylward. “Better to strike the rear guard than to feather a shaft in the earth.”
“Loose quick and sharp when they come,” added another. “Let it be the eye to the string, the string to the shaft, and the shaft to the mark. By Our Lady! their banners advance, and we must hold our ground now if ever we are to see Southampton Water again.”
{{Alleyne}}, standing with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} sword drawn amidst the archers, saw a long toss and heave of the glittering squadrons. Then the front ranks began to surge slowly forward, to trot, to canter, to gallop, and in an instant the whole vast array was hurtling onward, line after line, the air full of the thunder of their cries, the ground shaking with the beat of their hoofs, the valley choked with the rushing torrent of steel, topped by the waving plumes, the slanting spears and the fluttering banderoles. On they swept over the level and up to the slope, ere they met the blinding storm of the English arrows. Down went the whole ranks in a whirl of mad confusion, horses plunging and kicking, bewildered soldiers falling, rising, staggering on or back, while ever new lines of riders came spurring through the gaps and urged their chargers up the fatal slope. All around {{ him(Alleyne) }} {{Alleyne}} could hear the stern, short orders of the masters, while the air was filled with the keen twanging of the strings and the swish and patter of the shafts. Right across the foot of the hill there had sprung up a long wall of struggling horses and stricken people, which ever grew and heightened as fresh squadrons poured on the attack. One young knight on a gray jennet leaped over his fallen comrades and galloped swiftly up the hill, shrieking loudly upon Saint James, ere he fell within a spear-length of the English line, with the feathers of arrows thrusting out from every crevice and joint of his armor. So for five long minutes the gallant horsemen of Spain and of France strove ever and again to force a passage, until the wailing note of a bugle called them back, and they rode slowly out of bow-shot, leaving their best and their bravest in the ghastly, blood-mottled heap behind them.
But there was little rest for the victors. Whilst the knights had charged them in front the slingers had crept round upon either flank and had gained a footing upon the cliffs and behind the outlying rocks. A storm of stones broke suddenly upon the defenders, who, drawn up in lines upon the exposed summit, offered a fair mark to their hidden foes. Johnston, the old archer, was struck upon the temple and fell dead without a groan, while fifteen of {{ hisd(John) }} line and six of the men-at-arms were struck down at the same moment. The others lay on their faces to avoid the deadly hail, while at each side of the plateau a fringe of bowmen exchanged shots with the slingers and crossbowmen among the rocks, aiming mainly at those who had swarmed up the cliffs, and bursting into laughter and cheers when a well-aimed shaft brought one of their opponents toppling down from a lofty perch.
“I think, {{Nigel}},” said {{ Sirr(Oliver) }} {{Oliver}}, striding across to the little knight, “that we should all acquit ourselves better had we our none-meat, for the sun is high in the heaven.”
“By Saint Paul!” quoth {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, plucking the patch from {{ hisd(Nigel) }} eye, “I think that I am now clear of my vow, for this Spanish knight was a person from whom much honor might be won. Indeed, {{ he(Sebastian) }} was a very worthy {{ gentleman(Sebastian) }}, of good courage, and great hardiness, and it grieves me that {{ he(Sebastian) }} should have come by such a hurt. As to what you say of food, {{Oliver}}, it is not to be thought of, for we have nothing with us upon the hill.”
“{{Nigel}}!” cried {{ Sirr(Simon) }} {{Simon}} Burley, hurrying up with consternation upon {{ hisd(Simon) }} face, “Aylward tells me that there are not ten-score arrows left in all their sheaves. See! they are springing from their horses, and cutting their sollerets that they may rush upon us. Might we not even now make a retreat?”
“My soul will retreat from my body first!” cried the little knight. “Here I am, and here I bide, while God gives me strength to lift a sword.”
“And so say I!” shouted {{ Sirr(Oliver) }} {{Oliver}}, throwing {{ hisd(Oliver) }} mace high into the air and catching it again by the handle.
“To your arms, men!” roared {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}. “Shoot while you may, and then out sword, and let us live or die together!”
<h2>Chapter XXXVII How the White Company Came to be Disanded</h2>
Then up rose from the hill in the rugged Cantabrian valley a sound such as had not been heard in those parts before, nor was again, until the streams which rippled amid the rocks had been frozen by over four hundred winters and thawed by as many returning springs. Deep and full and strong it thundered down the ravine, the fierce battle-call of a warrior race, the last stern welcome to whoso should join with them in that world-old game where the stake is death. Thrice it swelled forth and thrice it sank away, echoing and reverberating amidst the crags. Then, with set faces, the Company rose up among the storm of stones, and looked down upon the thousands who sped swiftly up the slope against them. Horse and spear had been set aside, but on foot, with sword and battle-axe, their broad shields slung in front of them, the chivalry of Spain rushed to the attack.
And now arose a struggle so fell, so long, so evenly sustained, that even now the memory of it is handed down amongst the Cantabrian mountaineers and the ill-omened knoll is still pointed out by fathers to their children as the “Altura de los Inglesos,” where those from across the sea fought the great fight with the knights of the south. The last arrow was quickly shot, nor could the slingers hurl their stones, so close were friend and foe. From side to side stretched the thin line of the English, lightly armed and quick-footed, while against it stormed and raged the pressing throng of fiery Spaniards and of gallant Bretons. The clink of crossing sword-blades, the dull thudding of heavy blows, the panting and gasping of the weary and the wounded , all rose together in a wild, long-drawn note, which swelled upwards to the ears of the wondering peasants who looked down from the edges of the cliffs upon the swaying turmoil of the battle beneath them. Back and forward reeled the leopard banner, now borne up the slope by the rush and weight of the onslaught, now pushing downwards again as {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, Burley, and Black {{Simon}} with their veterans flung themselves madly into the fray. {{Alleyne}}, at {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ lord(Nigel) }}'s right hand, found {{ himself(Alleyne) }} swept hither and thither in the desperate struggle, exchanging savage thrusts one instant with a Spanish cavalier, and the next torn away by the whirl of fighters and dashed up against some new antagonist. To the right {{ Sirr(Oliver) }} {{Oliver}}, Aylward, Hordle {{John}}, and the archers of the Company fought furiously against the monkish Knights of Santiago, who were led up the hill by their prior—a great, deep-chested {{ man(Santiago) }}, who wore a brown monastic habit over {{ hisd(Santiago) }} suit of mail. Three archers {{ he(Santiago) }} slew in three giant strokes, but {{ Sirr(Oliver) }} {{Oliver}} flung {{ hisd(Oliver) }} arms round {{ him(Santiago) }}, and the two, staggering and straining, reeled backwards and fell, locked in each other's grasp, over the edge of the steep cliff which flanked the hill. In vain {{ hisd(Santiago) }} knights stormed and raved against the thin line which barred their path: the sword of Aylward and the great axe of {{John}} gleamed in the forefront of the battle and huge jagged pieces of rock, hurled by the strong arms of the bowmen, crashed and hurtled amid their ranks. Slowly they gave back down the hill, the archers still hanging upon their skirts, with a long litter of writhing and twisted figures to mark the course which they had taken. At the same instant the Welsh upon the left, led on by the Scotch {{ earl(Thomas) }}, had charged out from among the rocks which sheltered them, and by the fury of their outfall had driven the Spaniards in front of them in headlong flight down the hill. In the centre only things seemed to be going ill with the defenders. Black {{Simon}} was down—dying, as {{ he(Simon) }} would wish to have died, like a grim old wolf in its lair with a ring of {{ hisd(Simon) }} slain around {{ him(Simon) }}. Twice {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} had been overborne, and twice {{Alleyne}} had fought over {{ him(Nigel) }} until {{ hed(Nigel) }} had staggered to {{ hisd(Nigel) }} feet once more. Burley lay senseless, stunned by a blow from a mace, and half of the soldiers lay littered upon the ground around {{ him(Simon) }}. {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}'s shield was broken, {{ hisd(Nigel) }} crest shorn, {{ hisd(Nigel) }} armor cut and smashed, and the vizor torn from {{ hisd(Nigel) }} helmet; yet {{ he(Nigel) }} sprang hither and thither with light foot and ready hand, engaging two Bretons and a Spaniard at the same instant—thrusting, stooping, dashing in, springing out—while {{Alleyne}} still fought by {{ hisd(Nigel) }} side, stemming with a handful of English the fierce tide which surged up against them. Yet it would have fared ill with them had not the archers from either side closed in upon the flanks of the attackers, and pressed them very slowly and foot by foot down the long slope, until they were on the plain once more, where their fellows were already rallying for a fresh assault.
But terrible indeed was the cost at which the last had been repelled. Of the three hundred and seventy fighters who had held the crest, one hundred and seventy-two were left standing, many of whom were sorely wounded and weak from loss of blood. {{ Sirr(Oliver) }} {{Oliver}} Buttesthorn, {{ Sirr(Richard) }} {{Richard}} Causton, {{ Sirr(Simon) }} {{Simon}} Burley, Black {{Simon}}, Johnston, a hundred and fifty archers, and forty-seven  men-at-arms had fallen, while the pitiless hail of stones was already whizzing and piping once more about their ears, threatening every instant to further reduce their numbers.
{{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} looked about {{ him(Nigel) }} at {{ hisd(Nigel) }} shattered ranks, and {{ hisd(Nigel) }} face flushed with a soldier's pride.
“By St. Paul!” {{ he(Nigel) }} cried, “I have fought in many a little bickering, but never one that I would be more loth to have missed than this. But you are wounded, {{Alleyne}}?”
“It is nought,” answered {{ hisd(Nigel) }} squire, stanching the blood which dripped from a sword-cut across {{ hisd(Nigel) }} forehead.
“These nobles of Spain seem to be most courteous and worthy people. I see that they are already forming to continue this debate with us. Form up the archers two deep instead of four. By my faith! some very brave souls have gone from among us. Aylward, you are a trusty soldier, for all that your shoulder has never felt accolade, nor your heels worn the gold spurs. Do you take charge of the right; I will hold the centre, and you, my {{ Lord(Thomas) }} of Angus, the left.”
“Ho! for {{ Sirr(Samkin) }} {{Samkin}} Aylward!” cried a rough voice among the archers, and a roar of laughter greeted their new leader.
“By my hilt!” said the old bow{{ man(Samkin) }}, “I never thought to lead a wing in a stricken field. Stand close, camarades, for, by these finger-bones! we must play the warrior this day.”
“Come hither, {{Alleyne}},” said {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}, walking back to the edge of the cliff which formed the rear of their position. “And you, Norbury,” {{ he(Nigel) }} continued, beckoning to the squire of {{ Sirr(Oliver) }} {{Oliver}}, “do you also come here.”
The two squires hurried across to {{ him(Nigel) }}, and the three stood looking down into the rocky ravine which lay a hundred and fifty feet beneath them.
“The {{ prince(Edward) }} must hear of how things are with us,” said the knight. “Another onfall we may withstand, but they are many and we are few, so that the time must come when we can no longer form line across the hill. Yet if help were brought us we might hold the crest until it comes. See yonder horses which stray among the rocks beneath us?”
“I see them, my fair {{ lord(Nigel) }}.”
“And see yonder path which winds along the hill upon the further end of the valley?”
“I see it.”
“Were you on those horses, and riding up yonder track, steep and rough as it is, I think that ye might gain the valley beyond. Then on to the {{ prince(Edward) }}, and tell {{ him(Edward) }} how we fare.”
“But, my fair {{ lord(Nigel) }}, how can we hope to reach the horses?” asked Norbury.
“Ye cannot go round to them, for they would be upon ye ere ye could come to them. Think ye that ye have heart enough to clamber down this cliff?”
“Had we but a rope.”
“There is one here. It is but one hundred feet long, and for the rest ye must trust to God and to your fingers. Can you try it, {{Alleyne}}?”
“With all my heart, my dear {{ lord(Nigel) }}, but how can I leave you in such a strait?”
“Nay, it is to serve me that ye go. And you, Norbury?”
The silent squire said nothing, but {{ he(John) }} took up the rope, and, having examined it, {{ he(John) }} tied one end firmly round a projecting rock. Then {{ he(John) }} cast off {{ hisd(John) }} breast-plate, thigh pieces, and greaves, while {{Alleyne}} followed {{ hisd(John) }} example.
“Tell Chandos, or Calverley, or Knolles, should the {{ prince(Edward) }} have gone forward,” cried {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}. “Now may God speed ye, for ye are brave and worthy.”
It was, indeed, a task which might make the heart of the bravest sink. The thin cord dangling down the face of the brown cliff seemed from above to reach little more than half-way down it. Beyond stretched the rugged rock, wet and shining, with a green tuft here and there thrusting out from it, but little sign of ridge or foothold. Far below the jagged points of the boulders bristled up, dark and menacing. Norbury tugged thrice with all {{ hisd(John) }} strength upon the cord, and then lowered {{ himself(John) }} over the edge, while a hundred anxious faces peered over at {{ him(John) }} as {{ he(John) }} slowly clambered downwards to the end of the rope. Twice {{ he(John) }} stretched out {{ hisd(John) }} foot, and twice {{ he(John) }} failed to reach the point at which {{ he(John) }} aimed, but even as {{ he(John) }} swung {{ himself(John) }} for a third effort a stone from a sling buzzed like a wasp from amid the rocks and struck {{ him(John) }} full upon the side of {{ hisd(John) }} head. {{ Hisd(John) }} grasp relaxed, {{ hisd(John) }} feet slipped, and in an instant {{ he(John) }} was a crushed and mangled corpse upon the sharp ridges beneath {{ him(John) }}.
“If I have no better fortune,” said {{Alleyne}}, leading {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} aside. “I pray you, my dear {{ lord(Nigel) }}, that you will give my humble service to the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}}, and say to {{ her(Maude) }} that I was ever {{ herd(Maude) }} true servant and most unworthy cavalier.”
The old knight said no word, but {{ he(Nigel) }} put a hand on either shoulder, and kissed {{ hisd(Nigel) }} squire, with the tears shining in {{ hisd(Nigel) }} eyes. {{Alleyne}} sprang to the rope, and sliding swiftly down, soon found {{ himself(Alleyne) }} at its extremity. From above it seemed as though rope and cliff were well-nigh touching, but now, when swinging a hundred feet down, the squire found that {{ he(Alleyne) }} could scarce reach the face of the rock with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} foot, and that it was as smooth as glass, with no resting-place where a mouse could stand. Some three feet lower, however, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} eye lit upon a long jagged crack which slanted downwards, and this {{ he(Alleyne) }} must reach if {{ he(Alleyne) }} would save not only {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} own poor life, but that of the eight-score soldiers above {{ him(Alleyne) }}. Yet it were madness to spring for that narrow slit with nought but the wet, smooth rock to cling to. {{ He(Alleyne) }} swung for a moment, full of thought, and even as {{ he(Alleyne) }} hung there another of the hellish stones sang through {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} curls, and struck a chip from the face of the cliff. Up {{ he(Alleyne) }} clambered a few feet, drew up the loose end after {{ him(Alleyne) }}, unslung {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} belt, held on with knee and with elbow while {{ he(Alleyne) }} spliced the long, tough leathern belt to the end of the cord: then lowering {{ himself(Alleyne) }} as far as {{ he(Alleyne) }} could go, {{ he(Alleyne) }} swung backwards and forwards until {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} hand reached the crack, when {{ he(Alleyne) }} left the rope and clung to the face of the cliff. Another stone struck {{ him(Alleyne) }} on the side, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} heard a sound like a breaking stick, with a keen stabbing pain which shot through {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} chest. Yet it was no time now to think of pain or ache. There was {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ lord(Nigel) }} and {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} eight-score comrades, and they must be plucked from the jaws of death. On {{ he(Alleyne) }} clambered, with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} hand shuffling down the long sloping crack, sometimes bearing all {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} weight upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} arms, at others finding some small shelf or tuft on which to rest {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} foot. Would {{ he(Alleyne) }} never pass over that fifty feet? {{ He(Alleyne) }} dared not look down and could but grope slowly onwards, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} face to the cliff, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} fingers clutching, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} feet scraping and feeling for a support. Every vein and crack and mottling of that face of rock remained forever stamped upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} memory. At last, however, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} foot came upon a broad resting-place and {{ he(Alleyne) }} ventured to cast a glance downwards. Thank God! {{ he(Alleyne) }} had reached the highest of those fatal pinnacles upon which {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} comrade had fallen. Quickly now {{ he(Alleyne) }} sprang from rock to rock until {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} feet were on the ground, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} had {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} hand stretched out for the horse's rein, when a sling-stone struck {{ him(Alleyne) }} on the head, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} dropped senseless upon the ground.
An evil blow it was for {{Alleyne}}, but a worse one still for the one who struck it. The Spanish slinger, seeing the {{ youth(Alleyne) }} lie slain, and judging from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} dress that {{ he(Alleyne) }} was no common {{ man(Alleyne) }}, rushed forward to plunder {{ him(Alleyne) }}, knowing well that the archers above had expended their last shaft. {{ He(slinger) }} was still three paces, however, from {{ hisd(slinger) }} victim's side when {{John}} upon the cliff above plucked up a huge boulder, and, poising it for an instant, dropped it with fatal aim upon the slinger beneath {{ him(John) }}. It struck upon {{ hisd(slinger) }} shoulder, and hurled {{ him(slinger) }}, crushed and screaming, to the ground, while {{Alleyne}}, recalled to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} senses by these shrill cries in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} very ear, staggered on to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} feet, and gazed wildly about {{ him(Alleyne) }}. {{ Hisd(Alleyne) }} eyes fell upon the horses, grazing upon the scanty pasture, and in an instant all had come back to {{ him(Alleyne) }}—his mission, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} comrades, the need for haste. {{ He(Alleyne) }} was dizzy, sick, faint, but {{ he(Alleyne) }} must not die, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} must not tarry, for {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} life meant many lives that day. In an instant {{ he(Alleyne) }} was in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} saddle and spurring down the valley. Loud rang the swift charger's hoofs over rock and reef, while the fire flew from the stroke of iron, and the loose stones showered up behind {{ him(Alleyne) }}. But {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} head was whirling round, the blood was gushing from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} brow, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} temple, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} mouth. Ever keener and sharper was the deadly pain which shot like a red-hot arrow through {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} side. {{ He(Alleyne) }} felt that {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} eye was glazing, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} senses slipping from {{ him(Alleyne) }}, {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} grasp upon the reins relaxing. Then with one mighty effort, {{ he(Alleyne) }} called up all {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} strength for a single minute. Stooping down, {{ he(Alleyne) }} loosened the stirrup-straps, bound {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} knees tightly to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} saddle-flaps, twisted {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} hands in the bridle, and then, putting the gallant horse's head for the mountain path, {{ he(Alleyne) }} dashed the spurs in and fell forward fainting with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} face buried in the coarse, black mane.
Little could {{ he(Alleyne) }} ever remember of that wild ride. Half conscious, but ever with the one thought beating in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} mind, {{ he(Alleyne) }} goaded the horse onwards, rushing swiftly down steep ravines over huge boulders, along the edges of black abysses. Dim memories {{ he(Alleyne) }} had of beetling cliffs, of a group of huts with wondering faces at the doors, of foaming, clattering water, and of a bristle of mountain beeches. Once, ere {{ he(Alleyne) }} had ridden far, {{ he(Alleyne) }} heard behind {{ him(Alleyne) }} three deep, sullen shouts, which told {{ him(Alleyne) }} that {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} comrades had set their faces to the foe once more. Then all was blank, until {{ he(Alleyne) }} woke to find kindly blue English eyes peering down upon {{ him(Alleyne) }} and to hear the blessed sound of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} country's speech. They were but a foraging party—a hundred archers and as many men-at-arms—but their leader was {{ Sirr(Hugh) }} {{Hugh}} Calverley, and {{ he(Hugh) }} was not a {{ man(Hugh) }} to bide idle when good blows were to be had not three leagues from {{ him(Hugh) }}. A scout was sent flying with a message to the camp, and {{ Sirr(Hugh) }} {{Hugh}}, with {{ hisd(Hugh) }} two hundred retainers, thundered off to the rescue. With them went {{Alleyne}}, still bound to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} saddle, still dripping with blood, and swooning and recovering, and swooning once again. On they rode, and on, until, at last, topping a ridge, they looked down upon the fateful valley. Alas! and alas! for the sight that met their eyes.
There, beneath them, was the blood-bathed hill, and from the highest pinnacle there flaunted the yellow and white banner with the lions and the towers of the royal house of Castile. Up the long slope rushed ranks and ranks of fighters exultant, shouting, with waving pennons and brandished arms. Over the whole summit were dense throngs of knights, with no enemy that could be seen to face them, save only that at one corner of the plateau an eddy and swirl amid the crowded mass seemed to show that all resistance was not yet at an end. At the sight a deep groan of rage and of despair went up from the baffled rescuers, and, spurring on their horses, they clattered down the long and winding path which led to the valley beneath.
But they were too late to avenge, as they had been too late to save. Long ere they could gain the level ground, the Spaniards, seeing them riding swiftly amid the rocks, and being ignorant of their numbers, drew off from the captured hill, and, having secured their few prisoners, rode slowly in a long column, with drum-beating and cymbal-clashing, out of the valley. Their rear ranks were already passing out of sight ere the new-comers were urging their panting, foaming horses up the slope which had been the scene of that long drawn and bloody fight.
And a fearsome sight it was that met their eyes! Across the lower end lay the dense heap of people and horses where the first arrow-storm had burst. Above, the bodies of the dead and the dying—French, Spanish, and Aragonese—lay thick and thicker, until they covered the whole ground two and three deep in one dreadful tangle of slaughter. Above them lay the Englishmen in their lines, even as they had stood, and higher yet upon the plateau a wild medley of the dead of all nations, where the last deadly grapple had left them. In the further corner, under the shadow of a great rock, there crouched seven archers, with great {{John}} in the centre of them—all wounded, weary, and in sorry case, but still unconquered, with their blood-stained weapons waving and their voices ringing a welcome to their countrymen. {{Alleyne}} rode across to {{John}}, while {{ Sirr(Hugh) }} {{Hugh}} Calverley followed close behind {{ him(John) }}.
“By Saint George!” cried {{ Sirr(Hugh) }} {{Hugh}}, “I have never seen signs of so stern a fight, and I am right glad that we have been in time to save you.”
“You have saved more than us,” said {{John}}, pointing to the banner which leaned against the rock behind {{ him(John) }}.
“You have done nobly,” cried the old free companion, gazing with a soldier's admiration at the huge frame and bold face of the archer. “But why is it, my good {{ man(John) }}, that you sit upon this Spaniard.”
“By the rood! I had forgot {{ him(Diego) }},” {{John}} answered, rising and dragging from under {{ him(John) }} no less a person than the Spanish caballero, {{ Ddon(Diego) }} {{Diego}} Alvarez. “This {{ man(Diego) }}, my fair {{ lord(Hugh) }}, means to me a new house, ten cows, one bull—if it be but a little one—a grindstone, and I know not what besides; so that I thought it well to sit upon {{ him(Diego) }}, lest {{ he(Diego) }} should take a fancy to leave me.”
“Tell me, {{John}},” cried {{Alleyne}} faintly: “where is my dear {{ lord(Nigel) }}, {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} Loring?”
“{{ He(Nigel) }} is dead, I fear. I saw them throw {{ hisd(Nigel) }} body across a horse and ride away with it, but I fear the life had gone from {{ him(Nigel) }}.”
“Now woe worth me! And where is Aylward?”
“{{ He(Samkin) }} sprang upon a riderless horse and rode after {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} to save {{ him(Nigel) }}. I saw them throng around {{ him(Samkin) }}, and {{ he(Samkin) }} is either taken or slain.”
“Blow the bugles!” cried {{ Sirr(Hugh) }} {{Hugh}}, with a scowling brow. “We must back to camp, and ere three days I trust that we may see these Spaniards again. I would fain have ye all in my company.”
“We are of the White Company, my fair {{ lord(Hugh) }},” said {{John}}.
“Nay, the White Company is here disbanded,” answered {{ Sirr(Hugh) }} {{Hugh}} solemnly, looking round {{ him(Hugh) }} at the lines of silent figures. “Look to the brave squire, for I fear that {{ he(Alleyne) }} will never see the sun rise again.”
<h2>Chapter XXXVIII Of the Home-Coming to Hampshire</h2>
It was a bright July morning four months after that fatal fight in the Spanish barranca. A blue heaven stretched above, a green rolling plain undulated below, intersected with hedge-rows and flecked with grazing sheep. The sun was yet low in the heaven, and the red cows stood in the long shadow of the elms, chewing the cud and gazing with great vacant eyes at two horsemen who were spurring it down the long white road which dipped and curved away back to where the towers and pinnacles beneath the flat-topped hill marked the old town of Winchester.
Of the riders one was young, graceful, and fair, clad in plain doublet and hosen of blue Brussels cloth, which served to show {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} active and well-knit figure. A flat velvet cap was drawn forward to keep the glare from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} eyes, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} rode with lips compressed and anxious face, as one who has much care upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} mind. Young as {{ he(Alleyne) }} was, and peaceful as was {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} dress, the dainty golden spurs which twinkled upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} heels proclaimed {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} knighthood, while a long seam upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} brow and a scar upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} temple gave a manly grace to {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} refined and delicate countenance. {{ Hisd(Alleyne) }} comrade was a large, red-headed {{ man(John) }} upon a great black horse, with a huge canvas bag slung from {{ hisd(John) }} saddle-bow, which jingled and clinked with every movement of {{ hisd(John) }} steed. {{ Hisd(John) }} broad, brown face was lighted up by a continual smile, and {{ he(John) }} looked slowly from side to side with eyes which twinkled and shone with delight. Well might {{John}} rejoice, for was {{ he(John) }} not back in {{ hisd(John) }} native Hampshire, had {{ he(John) }} not {{ Ddon(Diego) }} {{Diego}}'s five thousand crowns rasping against {{ hisd(John) }} knee, and above all was {{ he(John) }} not {{ himself(John) }} squire now to {{ Sirr(Alleyne) }} {{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }}, the young Socman of Minstead lately knighted by the sword of the Black {{ Prince(Edward) }} {{ himself(Edward) }}, and esteemed by the whole army as one of the most rising of the soldiers of England.
For the last stand of the Company had been told throughout Christendom wherever a brave deed of arms was loved, and honors had flowed in upon the few who had survived it. For two months {{Alleyne}} had wavered betwixt death and life, with a broken rib and a shattered head; yet {{ youth(Alleyne) }} and strength and a cleanly life were all upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} side, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} awoke from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} long delirium to find that the war was over, that the Spaniards and their allies had been crushed at Navaretta, and that the {{ prince(Edward) }} had {{ himself(Edward) }} heard the tale of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} ride for succor and had come in person to {{ hisd(Edward) }} bedside to touch {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} shoulder with {{ hisd(Edward) }} sword and to insure that so brave and true a {{ man(Alleyne) }} should die, if {{ he(Alleyne) }} could not live, within the order of chivalry. The instant that {{ he(Alleyne) }} could set foot to ground {{Alleyne}} had started in search of {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ lord(Nigel) }}, but no word could {{ he(Alleyne) }} hear of {{ him(Nigel) }}, dead or alive, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} had come home now sad-hearted, in the hope of raising money upon {{ hisd(Nigel) }} estates and so starting upon {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} quest once more. Landing at London, {{ he(Alleyne) }} had hurried on with a mind full of care, for {{ he(Alleyne) }} had heard no word from Hampshire since the short note which had announced {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ brother(Simon) }}'s death.
“By the rood!” cried {{John}}, looking around {{ him(John) }} exultantly, “where have we seen since we left such noble cows, such fleecy sheep, grass so green, or one so drunk as yonder rogue who lies in the gap of the hedge?”
“Ah, {{John}},” {{Alleyne}} answered wearily, “it is well for you, but I never thought that my home-coming would be so sad a one. My heart is heavy for my dear {{ lord(Nigel) }} and for Aylward, and I know not how I may break the news to the {{ Lady(Mary) }} {{Mary}} and to the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}}, if they have not yet had tidings of it.”
{{John}} gave a groan which made the horses shy. “It is indeed a black business,” said {{ he(John) }}. “But be not sad, for I shall give half these crowns to my old mother, and half will I add to the money which you may have, and so we shall buy that yellow cog wherein we sailed to Bordeaux, and in it we shall go forth and seek {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}}.”
{{Alleyne}} smiled, but shook {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} head. “Were {{ he(Nigel) }} alive we should have had word of {{ him(Nigel) }} ere now,” said {{ he(Alleyne) }}. “But what is this town before us?”
“Why, it is Romsey!” cried {{John}}. “See the tower of the old gray church, and the long stretch of the nunnery. But here sits a very holy {{ man(hermit) }}, and I shall give {{ him(hermit) }} a crown for {{ hisd(hermit) }} prayers.”
Three large stones formed a rough cot by the roadside, and beside it, basking in the sun, sat the hermit, with clay-colored face, dull eyes, and long withered hands. With crossed ankles and sunken head, {{ he(hermit) }} sat as though all {{ hisd(hermit) }} life had passed out of {{ him(hermit) }}, with the beads slipping slowly through {{ hisd(hermit) }} thin, yellow fingers. Behind {{ him(hermit) }} lay the narrow cell, clay-floored and damp, comfortless, profitless and sordid. Beyond it there lay amid the trees the wattle-and-daub hut of a laborer, the door open, and the single room exposed to the view. The man ruddy and yellow-haired, stood leaning upon the spade wherewith he had been at work upon the garden patch. From behind him came the ripple of a happy woman's laughter, and two young urchins darted forth from the hut, bare-legged and towsy, while the mother, stepping out, laid her hand upon her husband's arm and watched the gambols of the children. The hermit frowned at the untoward noise which broke upon {{ hisd(hermit) }} prayers, but {{ hisd(hermit) }} brow relaxed as {{ he(hermit) }} looked upon the broad silver piece which {{John}} held out to {{ him(hermit) }}.
“There lies the image of our past and of our future,” cried {{Alleyne}}, as they rode on upon their way. “Now, which is better, to till God's earth, to have happy faces round one's knee, and to love and be loved, or to sit forever moaning over one's own soul, like a mother over a sick babe?”
“I know not about that,” said {{John}}, “for it casts a great cloud over me when I think of such matters. But I know that my crown was well spent, for the {{ man(hermit) }} had the look of a very holy person. As to the other, there was nought holy about {{ him(hermit) }} that I could see, and it would be cheaper for me to pray for myself than to give a crown to one who spent one’s days in digging for lettuces.”
Ere {{Alleyne}} could answer there swung round the curve of the road a fine carriage drawn by three horses abreast with a postilion upon the outer one. Very rich it was, with beams painted and gilt, wheels and spokes carved in strange figures, and over all an arched cover of red and white tapestry. Beneath its shade there sat a stout and elderly {{ lady(tweezers) }} in a pink cote-hardie, leaning back among a pile of cushions, and plucking out {{ herd(tweezers) }} eyebrows with a small pair of silver tweezers. None could seem more safe and secure and at {{ herd(tweezers) }} ease than this {{ ladyt(tweezers) }}, yet here also was a symbol of human life, for in an instant, even as {{Alleyne}} reined aside to let the carriage pass, a wheel flew out from among its fellows, and over it all toppled—carving, tapestry and gilt—in one wild heap, with the horses plunging, the postilion shouting, and the {{ ladyt(tweezers) }} screaming from within. In an instant {{Alleyne}} and {{John}} were on foot, and had lifted {{ herd(tweezers) }} forth all in a shake with fear, but little the worse for {{ herd(tweezers) }} mischance.
“Now woe worth me!” {{ she(tweezers) }} cried, “and ill fall on Michael Easover of Romsey! for I told him that the pin was loose, and yet he must needs gainsay me, like the foolish daffe that he is.”
“I trust that you have taken no hurt, my fair {{ ladyt(tweezers) }},” said {{Alleyne}}, conducting {{ herd(tweezers) }} to the bank, upon which {{John}} had already placed a cushion.
“Nay, I have had no scath, though I have lost my silver tweezers. Now, lack-a-day! did God ever put breath into such a fool as Michael Easover of Romsey? But I am much beholden to you, gentle sirs. Soldiers ye are, as one may readily see. I am myself a soldier's {{ daughter(tweezers) }},” {{ she(tweezers) }} added, casting a somewhat languishing glance at {{John}}, “and my heart ever goes out to the brave.”
“We are indeed fresh from Spain,” quoth {{Alleyne}}.
“From Spain, say you? Ah! it was an ill and sorry thing that so many should throw away the lives that Heaven gave them. In sooth, it is bad for those who fall, but worse for those who bide behind. I have but now bid farewell to one who hath lost all in this cruel war.”
“And how that, {{ lady(tweezers) }}?”
“{{ She(Maude) }} is a young {{ damsel(Maude) }} of these parts, and {{ she(Maude) }} goes now into a cloister. Great had been the rejoicing amid the Romsey {{ nun(Maude) }}s when the {{ Lady(Maude) }} {{Maude}} Loring had craved admission into their order—for was {{ she(Maude) }} not sole child and {{ heiress(Maude) }} of the old knight, with farms and fiefs which {{ she(Maude) }} could bring to the great abbey? Long and earnest had been the talks of the gaunt {{ lady(Maude) }} {{ abbess(Maude) }}, in which {{ she(Maude) }} had conjured the young novice to turn forever from the world, and to rest {{ herd(Maude) }} bruised heart under the broad and peaceful shelter of the church. And now, when all was settled, and when {{ abbess(Maude) }} and {{ mother(Maude) }} superior had had their will, it was but fitting that some pomp and show should mark the glad occasion. Hence was it that the good burghers of Romsey were all in the streets, that gay flags and flowers brightened the path from the nunnery to the church, and that a long procession wound up to the old arched door leading up the novice to these spiritual host. There was lay-{{ sister(Agatha) }} {{Agatha}} with the high gold crucifix, and the three incense-bearers, and the two-and-twenty garbed in white, who cast flowers upon either side of them and sang sweetly the while. But alas! for plots and plans when love and youth and nature, and above all, fortune are arrayed against them. Who is this travel-stained {{ youth(Alleyne) }} who dares to ride so madly through the lines of staring burghers? Why does {{ he(Alleyne) }} fling {{ himself(Alleyne) }} from {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} horse and stare so strangely about {{ him(Alleyne) }}? A sorry sight this for the gaunt {{ abbess(Maude) }}, an ill lesson too for the stainless two-and-twenty who have ever been taught that the way of nature is the way of sin. But {{Maude}} and {{Alleyne}} care little for this. A dank, cold air comes out from the black arch before them. Without, the sun shines bright and the birds are singing amid the ivy on the drooping beeches. Their choice is made, and they turn away hand-in-hand, with their backs to the darkness and their faces to the light.
Very quiet was the wedding in the old priory church at Christchurch, where {{ Father(Christopher) }} {{Christopher}} read the service, and there were few to see save the {{ Lady(Mary) }} Loring and {{John}}, and a dozen archers from the castle. The {{ Lady(Mary) }} of Twynham had drooped and pined for weary months, so that {{ herd(Mary) }} face was harsher and less comely than before, yet {{ she(Maude) }} still hoped on, for {{ herd(Maude) }} {{ lord(Nigel) }} had come through so many dangers that {{ she(Mary) }} could scarce believe that {{ he(Nigel) }} might be stricken down at last. It had been {{ herd(Mary) }} wish to start for Spain and to search for {{ him(Nigel) }}, but {{Alleyne}} had persuaded {{ her(Mary) }} to let {{ him(Alleyne) }} go in {{ herd(Mary) }} place. There was much to look after, now that the lands of Minstead were joined to those of Twynham, and {{Alleyne}} had promised {{ her(Mary) }} that if {{ she(Mary) }} would but bide with {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} {{ wife(Maude) }} {{ he(Alleyne) }} would never come back to Hampshire again until {{ he(Alleyne) }} had gained some news, good or ill, of {{ herd(Mary) }} {{ lord(Nigel) }} and lover.
The yellow cog had been engaged, with Goodwin Hawtayne in command, and a month after the wedding {{Alleyne}} rode down to Bucklershard to see if she had come round yet from Southampton. On the way {{ he(Alleyne) }} passed the fishing village of Pitt's Deep, and marked that a little creyer or brig was tacking off the land, as though about to anchor there. On {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} way back, as {{ he(Alleyne) }} rode towards the village, {{ he(Alleyne) }} saw that she had indeed anchored, and that many boats were round her, bearing cargo to the shore.
A bow-shot from Pitt's Deep there was an inn a little back from the road, very large and wide-spread, with a great green bush hung upon a pole from one of the upper windows. At this window {{ he(Alleyne) }} marked, as {{ he(Alleyne) }} rode up, that a {{ man(Nigel) }} was seated who appeared to be craning {{ hisd(Nigel) }} neck in {{ hisd(Nigel) }} direction. {{Alleyne}} was still looking up at {{ him(Nigel) }}, when a woman came rushing from the open door of the inn, and made as though she would climb a tree, looking back the while with a laughing face. Wondering what these doings might mean, {{Alleyne}} tied {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} horse to a tree, and was walking amid the trunks towards the inn, when there shot from the entrance a second woman who made also for the trees. Close at her heels came a burly, brown-faced {{ man(Samkin) }}, who leaned against the door-post and laughed loudly with {{ hisd(Samkin) }} hand to {{ hisd(Samkin) }} side, “Ah, mes belles!” {{ he(Samkin) }} cried, “and is it thus you treat me? Ah, mes petites! I swear by these finger-bones that I would not hurt a hair of your pretty heads; but I have been among the black paynim, and, by my hilt! it does me good to look at your English cheeks. Come, drink a stoup of muscadine with me, mes anges, for my heart is warm to be among ye again.”
At the sight of the {{ man(Samkin) }} {{Alleyne}} had stood staring, but at the sound of {{ hisd(Samkin) }} voice such a thrill of joy bubbled up in {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} heart that {{ he(Alleyne) }} had to bite {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} lip to keep {{ himself(Alleyne) }} from shouting outright. But a deeper pleasure yet was in store. Aylward at this order came shuffling forward amid the trees, and in an instant the two were clinging in each other's arms, laughing and shouting and patting each other in their delight; while old {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} came running with {{ hisd(Nigel) }} sword, under the impression that some small bickering had broken out, only to embrace and be embraced {{ himself(Nigel) }}, until all three were hoarse with their questions and outcries and congratulations.
On their journey home through the woods {{Alleyne}} learnt their wondrous story: how, when {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} came to {{ hisd(Nigel) }} senses, {{ he(Nigel) }} with {{ hisd(Nigel) }} fellow-captive had been hurried to the coast, and conveyed by sea to their captor's castle; how upon the way they had been taken by a Barbary rover, and how they exchanged their light captivity for a seat on a galley bench and hard labor at the pirate's oars; how, in the port at Barbary, {{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} had slain the Moorish captain, and had swum with Aylward to a small coaster which they had taken, and so made their way to England with a rich cargo to reward them for their toils. All this {{Alleyne}} listened to, until the dark keep of Twynham towered above them in the gloaming, and they saw the red sun lying athwart the rippling Avon. No need to speak of the glad hearts at Twynham Castle that night, nor of the rich offerings from out that Moorish cargo which found their way to the chapel of {{ Father(Christopher) }} {{Christopher}}.
{{ Sirr(Nigel) }} {{Nigel}} Loring lived for many years, full of honor and laden with every blessing. {{ He(Nigel) }} rode no more to the wars, but {{ he(Nigel) }} found {{ hisd(Nigel) }} way to every jousting within thirty miles; and the Hampshire youth treasured it as the highest honor when a word of praise fell from {{ him(Nigel) }} as to their management of their horses, or their breaking of their lances. So {{ he(Nigel) }} lived and so {{ he(Nigel) }} died, the most revered and the happiest {{ man(Nigel) }} in all {{ hisd(Nigel) }} native shire.
For {{ Sirr(Alleyne) }} {{Alleyne}} {{Edric}}{{ son(Alleyne) }} and for {{ hisd(Alleyne) }} beautiful {{ bride(Maude) }} the future had also naught but what was good. Twice {{ he(Alleyne) }} fought in France, and came back each time laden with honors. A high place at court was given to {{ him(Alleyne) }}, and {{ he(Alleyne) }} spent many years at Windsor under the second Richard and the fourth Henry—where {{ he(Alleyne) }} received the honor of the Garter, and won the name of being a brave soldier, a true-hearted {{ gentleman(Alleyne) }}, and a great lover and patron of every art and science which refines or ennobles life.
As to {{John}}, {{ he(John) }} took unto {{ himself(John) }} a village {{ girl-opp(John) }}, and settled in Lyndhurst, where {{ hisd(John) }} five thousand crowns made {{ him(John) }} the richest franklin for many miles around. For many years {{ he(John) }} drank {{ hisd(John) }} ale every night at the “Pied Merlin,” which was now kept by {{ hisd(John) }} friend Aylward, who had wedded the good widowto whom {{ he(Samkin) }} had committed {{ hisd(Samkin) }} plunder. The strong soldiers and archers of the country round used to drop in there of an evening to wrestle a fall with {{John}} or to shoot a round with Aylward; but, though a silver shilling was to be the prize of the victory, it has never been reported that any earned much money in that fashion. So they lived, these warriors, in their own lusty, cheery fashion—rude and rough, but honest, kindly and true. Let us thank God if we have outgrown their vices. Let us pray to God that we may ever hold their virtues. The sky may darken, and the clouds may gather, and again the day may come when Britain may have sore need of her children, on whatever shore of the sea they be found. Shall they not muster at the call?
